Recommended Acts for Fasting: Suhoor and Iftar
Explaining the virtues of delaying suhoor and hastening iftar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suhoor and iftar, explain the evidence from hadith, and distinguish the hukm level as sunnah muakkadah (emphasized recommended) rather than wajib.

Core Idea
Suhoor is blessed, not obligatory; hastening iftar is prophetic.

This passage collects four hadiths that establish the recommended practices for a fasting person regarding suhoor (pre-dawn meal) and iftar (breaking fast). The author introduces the chapter by explicitly stating that suhoor is not obligatory but contains blessing. The hadiths emphasize delaying suhoor until just before dawn and hastening iftar immediately after sunset, identifying these as characteristics of prophets. For Hanafis, these acts are elevated to the level of sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah) based on the strength of the evidence and the practice of the early community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended acts
From the hadiths, identify two key practices: taking suhoor (delayed until near dawn) and hastening iftar at sunset. Also note the additional practice of placing the right hand on the left in prayer mentioned in the last hadith.
The chapter heading explicitly states that suhoor is recommended, not obligatory.
2
Analyze the primary dalil
Focus on the first hadith (Ibn Umar): the imperative 'tasahharu' (take suhoor) is the key proof. In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning of an imperative is obligation (wujub), but it can be diverted to recommendation (nadb) by strong evidence (qarinah). Here, the chapter heading 'min ghayri ijab' (without obligation) and the absence of suhoor being a condition for valid fast serve as qarinah.
The same imperative appears in other contexts; interpreting it requires considering the overall evidence.
3
Understand the prophetic traits
Hadiths 2 and 4 explicitly state that delaying suhoor and hastening iftar are from the characteristics (akhlaq) of the prophets. This elevates these practices to a higher recommended status.
The third hadith clarifies the timing: one may eat until the adhan of Ibn Umm Maktum at true dawn (fajr sadiq).
4
Determine the hukm level
Based on the combination of evidence, Hanafis rule that suhoor and hastening iftar are sunnah muakkadah. They are not wajib, but they are strongly encouraged. Omitting them without a valid reason is makruh tanzihi (disliked).
Shafi'is also consider suhoor mustahabb but do not assign the same level of emphasis as Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب برکت السحور من غیر ایجاب. (۱) عن ابن عمر رضی الله عنهما قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «تسحروا فإن في السحور بركة» (متفق عليه). (۲) عن عائشة رضی الله عنها قالت: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «تعجيل الفطر وتأخير السحور من أخلاق النبيين» (سنن البيهقي). (۳) عن عائشة رضی الله عنها أن بلالاً كان يؤذن بليل فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «كلوا واشربوا حتى يؤذن ابن أم مكتوم فإنه لا يؤذن حتى يطلع الفجر» (متفق عليه). (۴) عن أبي الدرداء رضي الله عنه قال: من أخلاق النبيين: التبكير في الإفطار والإبلاغ في السحور ووضع اليمين على الشمال في الصلاة (مصنف ابن أبي شيبة).
Chapter on the blessing of suhoor without obligation. (1) Ibn Umar narrated: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Take suhoor, for in suhoor there is blessing.' (2) Aisha narrated: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Hastening iftar and delaying suhoor are from the characteristics of the prophets.' (3) Aisha narrated that Bilal used to give the adhan at night, so the Messenger of Allah said: 'Eat and drink until Ibn Umm Maktum gives the adhan, for he does not give the adhan until dawn breaks.' (4) Abu Darda said: From the characteristics of the prophets are: hastening iftar, delaying suhoor, and placing the right hand on the left in prayer.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a collection of hadith proofs establishing the recommended (mustahabb) practices for the fasting person. The author begins by affirming that suhoor is not obligatory but contains blessing. The first hadith from Ibn Umar (in Bukhari and Muslim) directly commands suhoor, but the command is interpreted as recommendation (nadb) rather than obligation due to other evidence. The second hadith from Aisha explicitly mentions that hastening iftar and delaying suhoor are from the character of prophets. The third hadith clarifies the timing: one may eat and drink until the second adhan of Ibn Umm Maktum, which occurs at true dawn (fajr sadiq). The fourth hadith from Abu Darda adds the placing of the right hand on the left in prayer as another prophetic trait. The Hanafi position is that delaying suhoor until just before fajr (but not after) and hastening iftar immediately after sunset are mustahabb, not wajib.

Key Terms
سحور (suhoor): The pre-dawn meal eaten by a Muslim intending to fast.
إفطار (iftar): The meal eaten to break the fast at sunset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Suhoor should be early and iftar should be delayed
B) Suhoor should be delayed and iftar should be hastened
C) Both should be done at midnight
D) Suhoor is forbidden before fajr

Key Takeaway
Suhoor is a blessed practice, not an obligation; hastening iftar is a prophetic trait.

Sickness and Travel: Concession to Break Fast
Ruling on sick and traveler breaking fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick person or traveler regarding fasting, and identify the Quranic and Hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Sickness and travel allow breaking fast; qada of missed days is wajib.

Fasting in Ramadan is an obligatory act, but the law provides concessions for those who face genuine hardship. This lesson focuses on two common excuses: sickness and travel. We will examine the direct Quranic permission and the prophetic practice that confirms this concession. Understanding the conditions under which one may break the fast is essential for correct application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling category
The text states that those who fear increase of illness or delay in recovery, and those who are traveling, may break the fast. Determine the hukm level: is it permissible, recommended, or obligatory to break?
It is a rukhsah (concession) – breaking is permissible, not sinful.
2
Examine the primary evidence
Read Quran 2:185: 'So whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then an equal number of other days.' Note the conditional structure and what it implies for the ruling.
The verse explicitly couples the concession with the obligation of qada.
3
Analyze the prophetic confirmation
The hadith of Ibn Abbas reports that the Prophet fasted during the conquest until reaching al-Kadid, then broke his fast. This shows that even without severe hardship, travel itself is a valid excuse.
The hadith is from Bukhari.
4
Apply the conditions
For sickness, the fear must be based on apparent signs or medical advice. For travel, the journey must be of sufficient length (typically 3 days/miles). List the conditions that must be present for the concession to apply.
Hanafi fiqh specifies details of travel distance and nature of illness.
5
Derive the obligation of qada
The verse commands 'iddatun min ayyamin ukhara' – a number of other days. Confirm that qada is wajib and that no other compensation (fidyah) is required for temporary excuses.
This distinguishes temporary from permanent inability.

فصل في عوارض الصوم. (۱۱۰۹) لمن خاف زيادة المرض أو بطء البرء. (١١١٠) ولحامل ومرضع خافت نقصان العقل أو الهلاك أو المرض على نفسها أو على الولد، ولدها كان أو رضيعاً. الدليل: قال تعالى: {فَمَن كَانَ مِنكُم مَّرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَىٰ سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ} (البقرة: ١٨٥). وعن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم خرج إلى مكة في رمضان فصام حتى بلغ الكديد أفطر فأفطر الناس (رواه البخاري). وعن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما في قوله: {وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِينٍ} قال: كانت رخصة للشيخ الكبير والمرأة الكبيرة وهما يطيقان الصيام أن يفطرا ويطعما مكان كل يوم مسكيناً، والحبلى والمرضع إذا خافتا (رواه أبو داود). وعن جابر بن عبد الله رضي الله عنهما أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رأى رجلاً يظلل عليه والزحام عليه فقال: «ليس من البر الصيام في السفر» (رواه مسلم).
Chapter on impediments to fasting. (1109) For one who fears increase of illness or delay in recovery. (1110) And for a pregnant or breastfeeding woman who fears deficiency of intellect, or destruction, or illness upon herself or upon the child, whether it is her own child or a suckling. The evidence: Allah says: 'So whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then an equal number of other days' (Quran 2:185). And from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet went to Mecca in Ramadan and fasted until he reached al-Kadid, then he broke his fast and the people broke theirs (Bukhari). And from Ibn Abbas regarding the verse: 'And upon those who are able, a ransom of feeding a poor person' – he said: It was a concession for the old man and old woman who are able to fast, that they may break fast and feed a poor person for each day, and for the pregnant and breastfeeding if they fear (Abu Dawud). And from Jabir that the Prophet saw a man being shaded and crowded, and said: 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling' (Muslim).
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling (hukm) on the excuses that permit a person not to fast. It covers two categories: (1) a sick person who fears that fasting will worsen the illness or delay recovery, and (2) a pregnant or breastfeeding woman who fears harm to her own health or to the child's health, whether the child is her own or a foster child. The ruling is that they may break the fast and are obligated to make up the missed days later (qada). The text provides four proofs: First, Quran 2:185 explicitly allows breaking fast for the sick and traveler. Second, the hadith of the Prophet breaking fast during the conquest of Mecca shows that even without extreme hardship, travel is a valid excuse. Third, the hadith from Ibn Abbas explains that old people and pregnant/breastfeeding women have a concession to break fast and feed a poor person (fidyah) instead of fasting. Fourth, the hadith of Jabir emphasizes that fasting while traveling can be undesirable if it causes hardship. The Hanafi position is that for sickness, the fear must be based on apparent signs or doctor's advice; for pregnancy/breastfeeding, if the woman fears for herself or the child, she breaks fast and makes up days later, and if she fears only for the child, she still breaks and makes up days but also must feed a poor person (fidyah) according to some narrations. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that qada is required for all these cases.

Key Terms
مرض (marad): Illness that causes fear of harm if fasting; must be real, not imaginary.
سفر (safar): Journey of a specific distance (approx. 48 miles) that permits concessions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The traveler must experience extreme thirst or hunger
B) The traveler must be on a journey of sufficient length (e.g., 48 miles)
C) The traveler must be sick as well
D) The traveler must have the intention to travel for at least 10 days

Key Takeaway
Sickness and travel are valid excuses to break the fast, with qada required; the traveler's concession is based on the journey itself, not hardship.

Pregnancy and Breastfeeding: Fearing Harm to Mother or Child
Ruling for pregnant/breastfeeding women breaking fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a pregnant or breastfeeding woman who fears harm to herself or her child, and identify the evidence from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Pregnant and breastfeeding women may break fast if they fear harm; qada is wajib, and fidyah is recommended.

Pregnancy and breastfeeding are special conditions where fasting may pose genuine risks. This lesson examines the concession granted to such women, based on Quranic indications and explicit hadith. We will also explore the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the requirement of fidyah, and how the ruling distinguishes between fear for oneself and fear for the child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories and fear
The text mentions pregnant and breastfeeding women (whether the child is her own or a suckling). The fear can be for herself (deficiency of intellect, destruction, illness) or for the child. Determine the ruling in each subcase.
The fear may be based on apparent signs or medical advice.
2
Examine the primary evidence
Read the hadith of Ibn Abbas regarding the verse 'wa ala alladhina yutiqunahu fidya'. He explains that the concession (breaking and feeding) was for the old and for pregnant/breastfeeding if they fear. Note that this hadith is from Abu Dawud.
The verse initially allowed fidyah for all; later it was abrogated for the healthy but remained for the permanently unable.
3
Analyze the different Hanafi opinions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that qada suffices; no fidyah. Imam Muhammad says if the fear is only for the child, she must also feed a poor person. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The mufta bihi is qada only, but fidyah is recommended (mustahabb).
4
Apply the conditions and consequences
List the conditions: the woman must genuinely fear harm. If she fears for herself, qada only. If she fears for the child, qada plus recommended fidyah. If she fears for both, qada only.
The fidyah is not obligatory but highly recommended.
5
Distinguish from permanent inability
The text also mentions old age. For permanent inability (old age, chronic illness), fidyah alone suffices without qada. Ensure understanding of the difference.
This is based on the same hadith of Ibn Abbas during the period of transition.

Rulings for Pregnant and Breastfeeding Women on Fasting with Proofs
Details exemptions from fasting for pregnant/breastfeeding women with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for pregnant and breastfeeding women who fear harm from fasting, identify the key proofs from the Quran and hadith, and explain why only qada (make-up) is required, not fidyah.

Core Idea
Pregnant/breastfeeding women break fast if they fear harm; they must only make up later (qada), not pay fidyah.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on fasting exemptions for pregnant and breastfeeding women. The school permits breaking the fast when there is genuine fear of harm to the mother or child. We will trace the proofs from the Quran and hadith, and understand why the Hanafi school holds that only make-up fasting (qada) is required, rejecting the view that fidyah must also be paid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the specific ruling
The act is fasting during Ramadan by a pregnant or breastfeeding woman. The ruling: it becomes permissible (jā'iz) for her to break her fast if she fears harm to herself or the child. She must later make up the missed days (qada).
This is a concession (rukhsah), not an obligation to break.
2
Locate the primary proof (dalīl) from the Sunnah
The author cites a hadith from Anas ibn Mālik that 'Allah has removed the fast for the pregnant or breastfeeding woman.' This is found in Sunan al-Tirmidhī and Sunan Abī Dāwūd.
This hadith is the basis for the exemption.
3
Analyze the athar of Ibn ʿAbbās for the exact requirement
Ibn ʿAbbās said: 'The pregnant and breastfeeding woman breaks fast in Ramadan and makes up fasting and does not feed (i.e., no fidyah).' This athar is found in Muṣannaf ʿAbd al-Razzāq and explicitly states that only qada is required.
This settles the issue for the Hanafī school.
4
Understand the ikhtilāf (disagreement) with other schools
Some scholars, including Imām Aḥmad and some Shāfiʿīs, require fidyah in addition to qada. The Hanafī school relies on the explicit wording of the athar to reject fidyah.
The ruling is specific to the Hanafī madhhab.

Section on impediments to fasting. And destruction or illness upon themselves or upon her child, whether by lineage or breastfeeding... Commentary: There are four scenarios: [1] The pregnant woman fears that if she fasts, the child will suffer mental deficiency, or die, or become ill — she may break fast. [2] The breastfeeding woman fears similarly for the child... [3] The pregnant woman fears for herself... [4] The breastfeeding woman fears for herself... Whether her own child or a foster child, the ruling is the same. Proof (1): There is also the verse: 'And as for those who can fast with difficulty, a ransom of feeding a poor person.' It is learned that those who cannot bear it will pay ransom. The aged man is included, but according to Ibn Abbas, the pregnant and breastfeeding are also included. From Ibn Abbas: 'And as for those who can fast with difficulty...' he said it was a concession for the old man and old woman who can bear fasting but break and feed a poor person each day, and for the pregnant and breastfeeding when they fear. This athar shows that they also pay fidyah each day. (2) Hadith from Anas ibn Malik... that Allah has removed half the prayer for the traveler and the fast for the pregnant or breastfeeding. This shows they are not required to fast. (3) They will make up later and not pay fidyah. The reason is the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'The pregnant and breastfeeding break in Ramadan and make up fasting and do not feed.' This shows they do not pay fidyah but make up.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi perspective on fasting exemptions for pregnant and breastfeeding women. The author first states the four scenarios: fear for the child's mental or physical harm, or fear for the woman's own health. The ruling applies whether the child is biological or foster. Three proofs are given: (1) Quran 2:184, interpreted by Ibn Abbas to include pregnant/breastfeeding, implying they may break fast and pay fidyah. However, the author then cites a second proof showing that they only need to make up later, not pay fidyah, based on a hadith and the athar of Ibn Abbas (in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). The Hanafi position, as presented here, is that they break fast and later make up (qada) without fidyah. This is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, as the earlier opinion of fidyah was abrogated. The passage does not explicitly state the hukm level (it is not fard), but it is permissible (mubah) with obligation to make up later.

Key Terms
مرضع (murḍiʿ): A woman who is breastfeeding a child
قضاء (qaḍā): The act of making up a missed obligatory fast outside of Ramadan
فدية (fidyah): A compensatory payment of feeding a poor person for each missed fast

Valid Fear for Breaking Fast: Its Basis
Defines the valid legal basis for breaking fast due to fear of harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two valid bases for presuming harm from fasting (experience and physician's report) and apply the condition of ghalabat al-zann.

Core Idea
Valid fear to break fast must be based on overwhelming presumption from experience or a reliable Muslim physician, not mere guesswork.

This passage clarifies the crucial criterion for when a fasting person may legitimately break the fast due to fear of harm. It distinguishes between vague anxiety and a legally recognized basis for action, grounding the ruling in both personal experience and professional medical opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
The passage states that breaking fast due to fear of harm is permissible (jāʾiz) only when the fear meets specific conditions. First, the fear must rise to the level of ghalabat al-zann (overwhelming presumption), not mere suspicion.
Ghalabat al-zann is a key usul concept: it means strong probability, sufficient for legal action even if not certainty.
2
Examine the two sources of ghalabat al-zann
The text specifies two valid sources: (1) repeated personal experience that fasting worsens or prolongs illness, and (2) the report of a skilled (hādhiq) and upright (ʿadl) Muslim physician. Both must create an overwhelming presumption of harm.
Experience requires repeated occurrence, not a single past incident. The physician must be both competent and morally reliable.
3
Understand the hadith proof
The author cites the hadith where the Prophet (saw) prescribed camel urine for people who fell ill in Medina. This shows that an experienced person's judgment is authoritative. Analogically, a physician's experienced opinion qualifies as a valid basis for fear.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari. Although the context is medical treatment, the principle of relying on experience is transferred to the case of fear of harm from fasting.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Now consider a person with a chronic illness: if he has repeatedly experienced that fasting worsens his condition, or if a trustworthy Muslim doctor advises him that fasting is harmful, he may break his fast. In both cases, ghalabat al-zann is established.
Without such a basis, breaking the fast would be invalid and sinful.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafiʿi) may accept a broader range of fears, such as the word of a non-Muslim physician or even a layperson's strong intuition. The Hanafi position is stricter, requiring a concrete basis from experience or a qualified Muslim doctor.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): in areas without a Muslim doctor, the Hanafi might not allow breaking fast, while other schools might.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (ا) اور خوف وہ معتبر ہے جو غلبہ بظن، یا تجربہ، یا مسلمان ماہر طبیب کے بتلانے سے حاصل ہو۔ تشریح : اس بات کا غالب گمان ہو کہ روزہ رکھنے سے بیماری بڑھ جائے گی، یادیر تک بیماری رہ جائے گی ، اس گمان کے بارے میں [1] یا تو بار بار کا تجربہ ہو کہ بیماری بڑھ جائے گی [۲] یا ماہر مسلمان طبیب ہو وہ کہے کہ بیماری بڑھ جائے گی ، یا بیماری دیر تک رہے گی تو اس کی بات مانی جائے گی ، خالی خولی گمان کافی نہیں ہے۔
كان مستندا الغلبة الظنّ بتجربة او اخبار طبيب مسلم حاذق عدل (1112)
وجه : (۱) اس حدیث سے استدلال کیا جاسکتا ہے کہ حکیم تجربہ کار ہو تب ہی عذر قبول کیا جائے گا . عن انس ان ناسا اجتووا في المدينة فامرهم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ان يلحقوا براعيه يعنى الابل فيشربوا من البانها و ابوالها فلحقوا براعيه فشربوا من البانها و ابوالها حتى صلحت ابدانهم فقتلوا الراعى و ساقوا الابل ( بخاری شریف، باب الدواء با بوال الابل ج ۱۰۰۶ نمبر ۶ ۵۶۸ ) اس حدیث سے پتہ چلتا ہے کہ حضور نے پیشاب پینے کا اشارہ فرمایا، کیونکہ آپ متجرب حکیم تھے
Translation: (a) And the fear that is considered valid is that which is obtained by overwhelming presumption (ghalabat al-zann), or by experience, or on the report of a skilled and upright Muslim physician. Commentary: It should be the dominant presumption that fasting will worsen the illness or prolong it. Regarding this presumption: [1] either there is repeated experience that the illness will worsen, [2] or a skilled Muslim physician says it will worsen or last long — his word is accepted; mere baseless assumption is not enough. 'It is based on overwhelming presumption by experience or on the report of a skilled, upright Muslim physician (1112).' Proof (1): This hadith can be used as evidence that a physician must be experienced for the excuse to be accepted. From Anas: Some people fell ill in Medina, so the Prophet (saw) ordered them to join his shepherd (meaning with the camels) and drink from their milk and urine. They did so until their bodies became healthy, then they killed the shepherd and took the camels. This hadith shows that the Prophet indicated drinking urine because he was an experienced physician.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof clarifying the valid basis for fearing harm from fasting. The matn states that the fear must be based on ghalabat al-zann (overwhelming presumption) arising from two sources: (1) repeated personal experience that fasting will worsen the illness, or (2) the report of a Muslim physician who is skilled (hādhiq) and upright (ʿadl). Mere subjective worry or guesswork is insufficient. The author then provides a hadith (from Bukhari) where the Prophet (saw), acting as an experienced physician, prescribed camel urine for treatment. This indicates that the judgment of an experienced person is authoritative. In the Hanafi school, this principle is applied to allow breaking fast when a reliable physician or prior experience indicates harm.

Key Terms
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-ẓann): Overwhelming presumption or strong probability, not mere suspicion

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The testimony of two eyewitnesses
D) A fatwa from a scholar

Key Takeaway
Valid fear to break fast must be based on ghalabat al-zann from repeated experience or the report of a skilled upright Muslim physician.

Breaking Fast Due to Severe Thirst or Hunger
Ruling with proof for breaking fast due to intense thirst/hunger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking fast due to severe thirst or hunger and cite the primary evidence.

Core Idea
Severe thirst or hunger risking death permits breaking fast; only qada required.

Fasting in Ramadan is a pillar, but Islamic law considers human necessity. This passage addresses a critical scenario: when thirst or hunger becomes so intense that death is feared. It provides the Hanafi ruling and the prophetic evidence that justifies breaking the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that for one who experiences severe thirst or hunger fearing death, it is permissible to break the fast.
The ruling is expressed as 'jā'iz' (permissible).
2
Locate the first proof
The author cites a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah where the Prophet said fasting while traveling with hardship is not righteousness.
This is evidence that severe hardship (like thirst) makes fasting undesirable and breaking it permissible.
3
Analyze the second proof
A hadith from Ibn Abbas shows the Prophet himself broke fast on a journey due to thirst, setting a precedent.
The Prophet raised water to his hands to make it visible, emphasizing the permission.
4
Understand the third proof
A statement from the Tabi'i 'Ata' ibn Abi Rabah indicates that one may break fast for any illness, which includes severe thirst/hunger as a form of illness.
This broadens the grounds for breaking fast beyond travel.
5
Determine the ruling's consequences
The Hanafi school holds that breaking fast in such cases requires only qada (makeup), not kaffarah, because the act was permissible.
Kaffarah is only for deliberate, invalidating acts without excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمن حصل له عطش شديد او جوع يخاف منه الهلاک. (1112) وجه : (۱) سخت پیاس میں آپ نے روزہ توڑنے کا مشورہ دیا۔ عن جابر ابن عبد الله قال كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في سفر فرأى زحاما و رجلا قد ظلل عليه فقال ما هذا ؟ فقالوا صائم فقال ليس من البر الصوم في السفر ( بخاری شریف، باب قول النبی من ظلل علیه واشتد الحر ليس من البر الصيام في السفر ،ص ۳۱۳ نمبر ۱۹۴۶) (۲) عن ابن عباس قال خرج رسول الله من المدينة الى مكة فصام حتى بلغ عسفان ثم دعا بماء فرفعه الى يديه ليراه الناس فافطر حتى قدم مكة ، و ذالك في رمضان و كان ابن عباس يقول قد صام رسول الله و افطر فمن شاء صام و من شاء افطر . (بخاری شریف، باب من افطر فی السفر ليراه الناس ،ص ۱۳۱۳، نمبر ۱۹۴۸/ ابوداود شریف، باب التاجر يفطر ،ص ۱۳۴۹، نمبر ۲۴۰۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ سخت پیاس کی وجہ سے روزہ توڑ دیا۔ (۳) اور سخت مرضوں سے روزہ توڑ سکتا ہے اس کی دلیل یہ قول تابعی ہے۔ قلت لعطاء : من اى وجع يفطر في رمضان ؟ قال منه كله قلت يصوم حتى اذا ..... افطـر ؟ قال نعم كما قال الله - (مصنف عبد الرزاق، باب ما يفطر منه من الوجع ، ج رابع ، ص ۱۶۹ نمبر ۷۵۹۸ ) اس قول تابعی میں ہے کہ ہر بیماری سے افطار کر سکتا ہے۔
And for one who experiences severe thirst or hunger from which he fears destruction. (1112) Proof: (1) In severe thirst, the Prophet advised breaking fast. From Jabir ibn Abdullah: The Messenger of Allah was on a journey and saw a crowd and a man with a shade over him. He asked, 'What is this?' They said, 'A fasting person.' He said, 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling.' (2) From Ibn Abbas: The Messenger of Allah left Medina for Mecca and fasted until Asfan, then called for water and raised it to his hands so the people could see, then broke his fast until he reached Mecca, and that was in Ramadan. Ibn Abbas said: The Messenger of Allah both fasted and broke, so whoever wishes may fast and whoever wishes may break. (3) One can break fast due to severe illness; the evidence is the saying of a Tabi'i: I said to 'Ata': From which ailment does one break fast in Ramadan? He said: From any of it. I said: Does he fast until he... breaks? He said: Yes, as Allah said.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof for breaking the fast due to severe thirst or hunger that threatens death. The matn clearly states that if one experiences severe thirst or hunger with fear of death, it is permissible to break fast. The author then gives three proofs: (1) A hadith from Jabir where the Prophet declared that fasting while traveling (when experiencing hardship) is not righteousness. This is directly relevant to thirst. (2) A hadith from Ibn Abbas showing that the Prophet himself broke his fast during a journey due to thirst, establishing the precedent. (3) A statement from 'Ata' ibn Abi Rabah, a Tabi'i, that one may break fast for any illness. This is broad and covers severe thirst/hunger as a form of illness. The Hanafi school uses these to rule that breaking fast is permissible and only qada is required, not kaffarah.

Key Terms
عطش شديد (ʿaṭash shadīd): Severe thirst that poses a risk of harm or death

Key Takeaway
Severe thirst or hunger that risks death makes it permissible to break the fast, with only qada required.

Ruling for Traveler: Fasting vs. Breaking (Preferences)
Ruling statement with expansion on preferences for traveler's fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a traveler's fasting and identify the three factors that determine whether fasting or breaking is preferred.

Core Idea
For a traveler, fasting is permissible and recommended if easy, but breaking is better for group harmony or shared expenses.

In this passage, we learn that both fasting and breaking fast are permissible for a traveler. However, the Hanafi school provides nuanced guidance: fasting is more beloved when it causes no harm and when the traveler's companions are not breaking fast. Conversely, if most companions are breaking or expenses are shared, breaking fast becomes superior to maintain group harmony. This lesson will help you understand the contextual factors that shift the preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Recognize that both fasting and breaking are jā'iz (permissible) for the traveler. This is the baseline.
2
Recognize the default preference
Note that fasting is aḥabb (more beloved) and mustaḥabb (recommended) if conditions are favorable.
3
List the conditions for fasting to be preferred
Identify the three conditions: (1) no harm from fasting, (2) most companions are not breaking, (3) not sharing expenses with companions.
The source text mentions these in the commentary.
4
Understand when breaking becomes superior
If any of the conditions are reversed (harm, most companions breaking, shared expenses), then breaking becomes afḍal to achieve group harmony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمسافر الفطر وصومه احب ان لم يضره ولم تكن رض (1113) تشریح : مسافر کے لئے روزہ رکھنا بھی جائز ہے اور افطار کرنا بھی جائز ہے۔ لیکن اگر روزہ رکھنے میں زیادہ مشقت نہ ہو تو روزہ رکھنا پسندیدہ ہے اگر اسے نقصان نہ ہو، اور اس کے زیادہ ساتھی افطار کرنے والے نہ ہوں، اور نہ خرچ میں مشترک ہوں، پس اگر ساتھی خرچ میں مشترک ہوں، یا زیادہ ساتھی افطار کرنے والے ہوں تو اس کے لئے افطار کرنا افضل ہے تا کہ جماعت کی موافقت حاصل ہو جائے۔
And for the traveler, breaking fast is permissible, and his fasting is more beloved if it does not harm him and there is not... (1113) Commentary: For the traveler, both fasting and breaking are permissible. But if fasting does not cause hardship, then fasting is recommended if it does not harm him, and if most of his companions are not breaking, and they are not sharing expenses. So if the companions share expenses, or most are breaking fast, then breaking is superior to achieve harmony with the group.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement (without explicit proof here) on the preference for a traveler regarding fasting. The matn states that the traveler may break fast, but fasting is more beloved (aḥabb) if it does not cause harm and the condition 'wa lam takun riḍ...' (probably 'wa lam takun rifqatan muftirīn' or similar) meaning if the companions are not breaking. The commentary explains that both options are permissible, but the preference depends on three factors: (1) if fasting causes no harm, it is recommended; (2) if most companions are fasting, fasting is recommended to avoid standing out; (3) if companions share expenses (e.g., in a journey where meals are common), breaking is superior to maintain group harmony. The Hanafi school balances individual ability with collective practice. This does not present a new proof but clarifies the established ruling.

Key Terms
مسافر (musāfir): A person undertaking a journey that qualifies for concessions (usually more than 48 miles or 3 days/march)
رفیق (rafīq): A companion or fellow traveler, especially one with whom expenses may be shared.
أحب (aḥabb): More beloved, indicating preference without obligation (mustaḥabb).
أفضل (afḍal): Superior or better, used when breaking is recommended over fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a traveler to fast or break, preferring fasting when easy and breaking when necessary for group harmony or shared expenses.

Breaking the Fast Due to Harm: The Hanafi Evidentiary Standard
Ruling on permissibility based on predominant opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the evidentiary standard (ghalabat al-zann) for breaking the fast due to fear of harm and identify the required conditions for qada'.

Core Idea
Breaking the fast due to severe thirst or illness is permissible when harm is likely, but qada' remains obligatory.

This passage addresses a critical question: when is it permissible to break a Ramadan fast due to severe thirst, hunger, or illness? The Hanafi school establishes a clear evidentiary standard—predominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann)—derived from personal experience or a reliable Muslim physician's report. The ruling balances the preservation of life against the obligation of fasting, and clarifies the consequences: qada' is required, not kaffarah. The author supports this with three proofs from the Sunnah and scholarly practice, emphasizing that the concession applies only when there is genuine fear of destruction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Evidentiary Basis
State the core ruling: it is permissible (jā'iz) to break the fast when one fears destruction from severe thirst, hunger, or illness. Note that the basis is predominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann), not certainty—this is a key Hanafi principle.
The basis is established by personal experience or the report of a skilled, upright Muslim physician.
2
List the Conditions for Permissibility
The fear of harm must be substantial—either from severe thirst, hunger, or a severe illness (e.g., diabetes, asthma). The standard of proof is ghalabat al-zann, which can come from one's own experience or a trustworthy physician's diagnosis.
The Urdu sharh adds that severe conditions like diabetes are included.
3
Examine the Proofs (Dalil)
Three proofs are cited: (1) The hadith of Jabir where the Prophet (ﷺ) said 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling'—implying that hardship permits breaking. (2) The hadith of Ibn 'Abbas where the Prophet himself broke his fast due to thirst and heat, demonstrating the practice. (3) The saying of 'Ata' that one can break for any illness, generalizing the principle from Quran 2:185.
These proofs establish that the concession is based on hardship, not mere convenience.
4
Determine the Thamarah (Legal Consequence)
The consequence is that qada' (making up the fast later) is obligatory, but no kaffarah (expiation) is required. If the excuse is temporary, qada' suffices; only permanent inability permits fidyah.
This distinguishes this ruling from cases of permanent chronic illness where fidyah may substitute.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Dispute
The Hanafi school accepts ghalabat al-zann; other schools may require yaqin (certainty) of harm. This affects who can break and on what basis.
This is an area of legitimate ikhtilaf, not a flaw in either school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في عوارض الصوم
كان مستندا الغلبة الظنّ بتجربة او اخبار طبيب مسلم حاذق عدل (۱۱۱۲) ولمن حصل له عطش شدید او جوع يخاف منه الهلاک.
ترجمه : (۱۱۱۲) جس کو سخت پیاس یا بھوک لگی ہو کہ جس سے ہلاکت کا خوف ہو ( اس کے لئے بھی افطار جائز ہے ) تشریح : سخت پیاس لگی ہو ، یا سخت بھوک لگی ہو کہ ہلاکت کا خطرہ ہو تو روزہ توڑنا جائز ہے، اس پر قضا لازم ہوگی ، کفارہ نہیں۔ اسی طرح شوگر کا سخت مریض ہو، دمے کا سخت مریض ہو، یا اس قسم کی سخت بیماری ہو تو روزہ رکھکر بھی توڑ نا جائز ہے، اور یہ بھی جائز ہے کہ رات سے ہی روزہ نہ رکھے لیکن شرط ہے کہ مرض سخت ہو۔
وجه سخت پیاس میں آپ نے روزہ توڑنے کا مشورہ دیا۔ عن جابر ابن عبد الله قال كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في سفر فرأى زحاما و رجلا قد ظلل عليه فقال ما هذا ؟ فقالوا صائم فقال ليس من البر الصوم في السفر ( بخاری شریف، باب قول النبی من ظلل علیه واشتد الحر ليس من البر الصيام في السفر ،ص ۳۱۳ نمبر ۱۹۴۶) (۲) عن ابن عباس قال خرج رسول الله الله من المدينة الى مكة فصام حتى بلغ عسفان ثم دعا بماء فرفعه الى يديه ليراه الناس فافطر حتى قدم مكة ، و ذالك في رمضان و كان ابن عباس يقول قد صام رسول الله علم و افطر فمن شاء صام و من شاء افطر . (بخاری شریف، باب من افطر فی السفر ليراه الناس ،ص ۱۳۱۳، نمبر ۱۹۴۸/ ابوداود شریف، باب التاجر يفطر ،ص ۱۳۴۹، نمبر ۲۴۰۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ سخت پیاس کی وجہ سے روزہ توڑ دیا۔ (۳) اور سخت مرضوں سے روزہ توڑ سکتا ہے اس کی دلیل یہ قول تابعی ہے۔ قلت لعطاء : من اى وجع يفطر في رمضان ؟ قال منه كله قلت يصوم حتى اذا ..... افطـر ؟ قال نعم كما قال الله - (مصنف عبد الرزاق، باب ما يفطر منه من الوجع ، ج رابع ، ص ۱۶۹ نمبر ۷۵۹۸ ) اس قول تابعی میں ہے کہ ہر بیماری سے افطار کر سکتا ہے۔
Chapter: Excuses for Fasting. The basis is the predominant opinion from experience or the report of a skilled, upright Muslim physician (1112). And for one who has severe thirst or hunger from which he fears destruction. Translation: (1112) For one who has severe thirst or hunger such that he fears destruction, it is permissible to break his fast. Explanation: If he has severe thirst or severe hunger such that there is risk of destruction, it is permissible to break the fast; qada' is obligatory, no kaffarah. Similarly, if he has a severe case of diabetes, severe asthma, or similar severe illness, it is permissible to break the fast even after having intended it, and it is also permissible not to fast from the night, provided the illness is severe. Evidence: The Prophet advised breaking the fast due to severe thirst. From Jabir ibn 'Abdullah: ... He said, 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling.' (Bukhari, no. 1946) (2) From Ibn 'Abbas: ... The Prophet broke his fast so that the people could see, and he said, 'Whoever wishes may fast and whoever wishes may break.' (Bukhari, no. 1948; Abu Dawud, no. 2404) This hadith mentions breaking fast due to severe thirst. (3) And that a severely ill person may break his fast is evidenced by the saying of a Tābi‘ī: I asked 'Ata': 'From what illness may one break fasting in Ramadan?' He said: 'From any of it.' I said: 'Should he fast until ... then break?' He said: 'Yes, as Allah said.' (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, no. 7598) This Tābi‘ī saying indicates that one may break fast due to any illness.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the permissibility of breaking the fast due to fear of harm. The author first states the basis: reliance on predominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) from either personal experience (tajribah) or the report of a skilled, upright Muslim physician. This establishes that mere assumption is insufficient; there must be a reasonable probability of harm. Then the specific case of severe thirst or hunger is given. The Urdu sharh adds that the same applies to severe illnesses like diabetes or asthma, and that one may either break the fast after starting or not intend to fast from the night. The author brings three proofs: (1) The hadith of Jabir where the Prophet (ﷺ) said 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling,' implying that severe hardship makes fasting undesirable. (2) The hadith of Ibn 'Abbas where the Prophet (ﷺ) himself broke his fast during a journey due to thirst and heat. (3) The saying of 'Ata' that any illness permits breaking the fast, based on the general principle in Qur'an 2:185. These proofs establish the Hanafi ruling that breaking the fast is permissible (jā'iz) when there is a genuine fear of harm, and qada' is required, but not kaffarah.

Key Terms
الغلبة الظنّ (ghalabat al-zann): Predominant opinion likely to be true, not mere conjecture; it is the evidentiary standard for permitting breaking the fast when based on experience or a reliable physician's report.
طبيب مسلم حاذق عدل (tabīb muslim ḥādhiq 'adl): A Muslim physician who is skilled (ḥādhiq) and upright ('adl) in character; his report is reliable for establishing the likelihood of harm.
عذر ('udhr): A legal excuse that permits breaking the fast, such as severe thirst, hunger, or illness that leads to fear of destruction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal basis for permitting the breaking of fasting due to severe thirst?
A) Certainty that harm will occur
B) Predominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) based on experience or a reliable Muslim physician's report
C) A mere possibility of harm
D) The feeling of thirst without severity

Key Takeaway
Breaking the fast due to severe thirst, hunger, or illness is permissible based on predominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann), but qada' remains obligatory for temporary excuses.

Fasting for the Traveler: Permissibility and Optimal Choice
Ruling with proofs on fasting while traveling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting while traveling, identify the conditions under which breaking is more virtuous, and apply the principle of group conformity.

Core Idea
A traveler may fast or break; fasting is better if it harms no one and the group fasts, but breaking is better for group harmony or shared expenses.

Fasting in Ramadan is a fundamental obligation, but what happens when you are traveling? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling: both fasting and breaking are permissible, but the best choice depends on circumstances. This lesson unpacks the conditions that tip the scale between fasting and breaking, drawing on the Qur'an and the Prophet's example. Understanding these details helps you apply the concession wisely and avoid common misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline ruling
Note that the passage starts by stating that it is permissible for the traveler to break the fast (al-fitr) and that fasting is more beloved (ahab). This establishes that both actions are valid but with a preference.
The term 'ahab' (more beloved) indicates a recommendation, not obligation.
2
List the conditions for fasting being more beloved
The passage lists three conditions: (1) no harm to the traveler, (2) the majority of companions are not breaking, and (3) they are not sharing expenses. If these hold, fasting is better.
These conditions are conjunctive (all must be met).
3
Identify when breaking becomes more virtuous
When the majority of companions are breaking or they share expenses, then breaking is more virtuous (afdal) to maintain group conformity (muwāfaqah li-l-jamā'ah).
This shows that social context can override the general preference for fasting.
4
Examine the evidence
The passage cites Qur'an 2:185 (option to break) and the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas (the Prophet fasted then broke at al-Kadid) to support the choice and the importance of following the easier path for the group.
The hadith demonstrates both that fasting is permissible and that breaking can be preferable for unity.

ولمسافر الفطر وصومه احب ان لم يضره ولم تكن عامة رفقته مفطرين ولا مشتركين فى النفقة فان كانوا مشتركين او مفطرين فالافضل فطره موافقة للجماعة.(۱۱۴ ا )
ترجمه : (۱۱۱۳) اور مسافر کے لئے روزہ نہ رکھنا جائز ہے، اور روزہ رکھنا پسندیدہ ہے اگر اسے نقصان نہ ہو، اور اس کے زیادہ ساتھی افطار کرنے والے نہ ہوں، اور نہ خرچ میں مشترک ہوں، پس اگر ساتھی خرچ میں مشترک ہوں، یا زیادہ ساتھی افطار کرنے والے ہوں تو اس کے لئے افطار کرنا افضل ہے تا کہ جماعت کی موافقت حاصل ہو جائے۔
تشریح : مسافر کے لئے روزہ رکھنا بھی جائز ہے اور افطار کرنا بھی جائز ہے۔ لیکن اگر روزہ رکھنے میں زیادہ مشقت نہ ہو تو روزہ رکھنا بہتر ہے۔ اور اگر جماعت کے ساتھ سفر کر رہا ہو اور اکثر ساتھی روزہ رکھ رہے ہوں تو اس کو بھی روزہ رکھنا چاہئے تا کہ جماعت کی موافقت ہو جائے۔ اور اگر اکثر ساتھی روزہ نہ رکھ رہے ہوں تو اس کو بھی روزہ نہ رکھنا چاہئے تا کہ جماعت کی موافقت ہو جائے۔ اور تیسری صورت یہ ہے کہ سب کا کھانا ایک ساتھ ہے، اور اکثر ساتھی روزہ نہیں رکھ رہے ہیں تو اس کو بھی روزہ نہ رکھنا چاہئے ، تا کہ وہ جب کھائیں تو انکے ساتھ یہ بھی کھا لیں اس کے لئے الگ سے سحری کا انتظام نہ کرنا پڑے۔
اصول: مسافر کے لئے دونوں کی گنجائش ہے لیکن مصلحت کا تقاضا کیا ہے وہ کرے۔
وجه (۱) یہ آیت ہے۔ فـمـن شهـد مـنـكـم الشهر فليصمه ومن كان مريضا او على سفر فعدة من ايام اخر يريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر - آیت ۱۸۵ اسۃ البقرۃ ۲) آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ مرض ہو یا سفر ہو تو روزہ توڑے گا اور دوسرے دنوں میں اس کی قضا کرے (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس أن رسول الله الله خرج الى مكة في رمضان فصام فلما بلغ الكديد أفطر فأفطر الناس . (بخاری شریف، باب اذا اصام آیا ما من رمضان ثم سافر بص ۱۳۱۲، نمبر ۱۹۴۴ را بود اود شریف ، باب التاجر يفطر، ص ۱۳۴۹، نمبر ۲۴۰۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ مشقت نہ ہو تو سفر میں روزہ رکھے، اور یہ بھی پتہ چلا کہ مصلحت ہو تو روزہ توڑ دے۔
For the traveler, breaking the fast (is permissible) and his fasting is more beloved if it does not harm him, and if the majority of his companions are not breaking their fast, and they are not sharing in expenses. If they are sharing expenses or are breaking fast, then it is more virtuous for him to break his fast in conformity with the group. (114) Translation: (1113) For the traveler, it is permissible not to fast, and fasting is recommended if it does not harm him, and the majority of his companions are not breaking fast, and they are not sharing expenses. If they are sharing expenses or are breaking fast, then it is more virtuous for him to break fast to conform with the group. Explanation: For the traveler, both fasting and breaking are permissible. But if fasting does not cause much hardship, then fasting is better. If he is traveling with a group and the majority are fasting, he should also fast to conform; if the majority are breaking, he should break to conform. A third scenario: if they share meals and the majority are breaking, he should break so that he can eat with them and not have to arrange a separate suhur. Principle: For the traveler, both options are available, but he should follow what is more beneficial. Evidence: (1) The verse: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast; and whoever is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days.' (al-Baqarah 2:185) This shows that if one is ill or traveling, he should break and make up later. (2) Hadith: Ibn 'Abbas narrated that the Prophet (ﷺ) set out for Mecca in Ramadan, fasting, then when he reached al-Kadid, he broke his fast, so the people broke. (Bukhari, no. 1944; Abu Dawud, no. 2404) This hadith shows that if there is no hardship, fast, but if it is beneficial, break.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the traveler's fasting. The author states that a traveler may either fast or break his fast; both are legally permissible. However, fasting is more beloved (ahab) if three conditions are met: (1) it does not harm him, (2) the majority of his companions are not breaking fast, and (3) they are not sharing expenses. If the majority are breaking or they share expenses, then breaking is more virtuous (afdal) to maintain group harmony. The Urdu sharh adds a third scenario: if the group shares meals and the majority break, the traveler should break to eat with them, simplifying arrangements. The principle underlying these rulings is that the traveler has the concession (rukhsah) to break, but the optimal choice depends on circumstances. The author then provides two proofs: (1) Qur'an 2:185, which grants the traveler the option to make up missed days later, and (2) the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) who both fasted and broke on journeys, showing the choice is tied to ease or benefit. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the traveler's fasting is valid and even preferable when no harm, but breaking may be better for social cohesion.

Key Terms
رفقته (rifqatihi): His travel companions; the group with whom he is traveling.
موافقة للجماعة (muwāfaqah li-l-jamā'ah): Conformity with the group; choosing the same practice (fasting or breaking) as the majority of companions to maintain unity and avoid discord.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when is it more virtuous for a traveler to break his fast rather than fast?
A) Whenever he feels any inconvenience
B) If his fasting would harm him or if his companions are breaking fast or they share expenses
C) Only if he is unable to fast at all
D) Always, because travelers should never fast

Key Takeaway
A traveler may choose to fast or break, but the optimal choice depends on personal harm, group practice, and shared expenses, with group conformity often outweighing individual preference.

Fasting While Traveling: Conformity with Companions
Explains when a traveler should fast or break fast based on companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended ruling for a traveler regarding fasting while with companions and identify the Quranic and hadith evidence that supports conformity.

Core Idea
The traveler may fast or break fast, but conformity with the group is recommended to maintain harmony.

This lesson examines the rulings on fasting during travel, particularly when one is part of a group. The Hanafi school holds that while both fasting and breaking fast are permissible, the recommended action is to align with the majority of one's companions to avoid discord. We will explore the textual evidence and the principle of maslahah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage describes three cases: when most companions are fasting, when most are not fasting, and when they share meals. For each, the recommended action is to conform.
Conformity (muwafaqah) is key.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The text states an usul (principle): the traveler has latitude, but should act according to maslahah (expediency). This justifies the recommendation to conform.
The principle is derived from the Quranic phrase 'Allah intends ease for you'.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Verse 2:185 grants a clear concession for the traveler to break fast, but also emphasizes ease. The Hanafi school uses this to support the option and the recommendation based on circumstances.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah regarding the best action.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The hadith of Ibn Abbas shows the Prophet fasting initially but breaking when it became expedient (al-Kadid). This demonstrates that conformity and ease are considerations.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Abu Dawud, showing its authenticity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في عوارض الصوم
ثمرة النجاح جلد ٢ عامة رفقته مُفطرين ولا مشتركين فى النفقة فان كانوا مشتركين او مفطرين فالافضل فطره موافقة للجماعة.(۱۱۴ ا ) كما رکھنا بہتر ہے۔ اور اگر جماعت کے ساتھ سفر کر رہا ہو اور اکثر ساتھی روزہ رکھ رہے ہوں تو اس کو بھی روزہ رکھنا چاہئے تا کہ جماعت کی موافقت ہو جائے۔ اور اگر اکثر ساتھی روزہ نہ رکھ رہے ہوں تو اس کو بھی روزہ نہ رکھنا چاہئے تا کہ جماعت کی موافقت ہو جائے۔ اور تیسری صورت یہ ہے کہ سب کا کھانا ایک ساتھ ہے، اور اکثر ساتھی روزہ نہیں رکھ رہے ہیں تو اس کو بھی روزہ نہ رکھنا چاہئے ، تا کہ وہ جب کھائیں تو انکے ساتھ یہ بھی کھا لیں اس کے لئے الگ سے سحری کا انتظام نہ کرنا پڑے۔
اصول: مسافر کے لئے دونوں کی گنجائش ہے لیکن مصلحت کا تقاضا کیا ہے وہ کرے۔
وجه (۱) یہ آیت ہے۔ فـمـن شهـد مـنـكـم الشهر فليصمه ومن كان مريضا او على سفر فعدة من ايام اخر يريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر - آیت ۱۸۵ اسورۃ البقرۃ ۲) آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ مرض ہو یا سفر ہو تو روزہ توڑے گا اور دوسرے دنوں میں اس کی قضا کرے (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس أن رسول الله الله خرج الى مكة في رمضان فصام فلما بلغ الكديد أفطر فأفطر الناس . (بخاری شریف، باب اذا اصام آیا ما من رمضان ثم سافر بص ۱۳۱۲، نمبر ۱۹۴۴ را بود اود شریف ، باب التاجر يفطر، ص ۱۳۴۹، نمبر ۲۴۰۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ مشقت نہ ہو تو سفر میں روزہ رکھے، اور یہ بھی پتہ چلا کہ مصلحت ہو تو روزہ توڑ دے۔
Section on the incidental matters of fasting. Thamaratun Najah Volume 2: The general rule for a traveler: if most of his companions are not fasting and they are not sharing expenses, but if they are sharing or are not fasting, then it is better for him to break the fast in conformity with the group. And if he is traveling with a group and most are fasting, he should also fast to conform with the group. And if most are not fasting, he should also not fast to conform. And a third case: if they all share meals and most are not fasting, then he should not fast so that he can eat with them and not need to arrange a separate pre-dawn meal. Principle: The traveler has latitude for both, but he should act according to what is expedient. Evidence: (1) This verse: 'So whoever among you is present during the month, let him fast. And whoever is ill or on a journey, then an equal number of other days. Allah intends ease for you and does not intend hardship for you.' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:185) From this verse it is understood that one who is ill or traveling may break the fast and make it up later. (2) A hadith: From Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) set out for Mecca in Ramadan and fasted until he reached al-Kadid where he broke his fast, so the people broke their fast. (Bukhari, Bab idha samma […] and Abu Dawud). This hadith indicates that if there is no hardship, he should fast while traveling, but if there is an expedient reason, he may break the fast.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its evidence regarding the traveler's fasting in relation to his companions. The matn states that if a traveler is with companions who are not fasting and they do not share expenses, the preferred action is to break the fast to maintain group harmony. The commentary expands on three scenarios: when most companions are fasting (recommended to fast), when most are not fasting (recommended to break fast), and when they share meals and most are not fasting (recommended to break fast to avoid separate arrangements). The underlying principle (usul) is that both fasting and breaking fast are permissible for a traveler, but one should choose based on maslahah (public benefit). The proofs are the Quranic verse 2:185 granting concession to the ill and traveler, and the hadith of Ibn Abbas where the Prophet initially fasted but broke fast at al-Kadid when he saw the state of the people. This demonstrates that while the basic rule is the concession, conformity with the group and avoidance of hardship are prioritized. In the Hanafi school, the traveler's choice is flexible, and following the majority of the group is recommended (mandub) to maintain unity.

Key Terms
عوارض الصوم ('awarid al-sawm): Incidental matters or auxiliary rulings related to fasting, such as travel, illness, and other exemptions.
موافقة الجماعة (muwafaqah al-jama'ah): Conforming to the practice of the group one is traveling with regarding fasting or breaking fast.
مسافر (musafir): A person who undertakes a journey of at least three days' distance (approx. 48 miles/ 77 km) or more, according to Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what should a traveler do if most of his traveling companions are fasting?
A) He must break fast to follow the concession.
B) He should also fast to conform with the group.
C) He must fast because fasting is always better.
D) He can choose based on his own convenience.

Key Takeaway
A traveler's fasting is flexible, but conformity with the group is recommended to maintain harmony and avoid hardship.

Continuity Not Required in Qada of Ramadan Fasts
Ruling with evidence on qada flexibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that continuity is not required in qada of Ramadan fasts, and explain the priority rule that the current Ramadan must be fasted before completing previous qada.

Core Idea
Qada of Ramadan fasts may be done separately; a current Ramadan must be fasted before previous qada.

This lesson clarifies a common question about making up missed Ramadan fasts: must they be done consecutively? And what if the next Ramadan arrives before qada is completed? Based on the Hanafi text from Thamaratun Najah, we will explore the permissibility of non-consecutive qada (tafriq) and the obligation to prioritize the current Ramadan, along with the absence of any fidyah for delay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the Hanafi position: continuity (tawali) is not a condition for qada of Ramadan fasts. One may fast them consecutively or with intervals.
The text explicitly says 'لا يشترط التتابع'.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the proof: the hadith of Ibn Umar (Dar Qutni) allowing either method, and the practice of Aisha (Bukhari) delaying qada until Sha'ban.
The hadith uses 'in sha'a farraqa wa in sha'a tabi'a' indicating optionality.
3
Understand the Priority Rule
When the next Ramadan arrives, its fasts must be performed first, before completing qada of the previous Ramadan.
This prevents the second Ramadan from being missed as well.
4
Note the Consequence of Delay
No fidyah is due for delaying qada until after the next Ramadan, but excessive delay without excuse is makruh and requires istighfar.
The athar of Ibrahim (incomplete in text) supports no fidyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يشترط التتابع في قضاء رمضان فاذا دخل رمضان آخر قدم صومه على القضاء ولا فدية عليه بالتأخير
ترجمه : (۱۱۶) اور قضا میں لگا تار روزہ رکھنے کی شرط نہیں ہے، پس اگر دوسرا رمضان آجائے تو اس کے روزے کو قضا پر مقدم کرے، اور ( دوسرے رمضان تک ) موخر کرنے سے فدیہ (واجب) نہیں
تشریح: مثلا دس روز رمضان کے روزے قضا ہوئے تھے تو یہ بھی جائز ہے کہ مسلسل دس روز روزے رکھ کر پورے کرے اور یہ بھی جائز ہے کہ دو روز رکھے پھر کچھ دنوں کے بعد چار روزے رکھے اور تفریق کر کے دس روز پورے کرے ۔ کیونکہ آیت میں مطلق ہے کہ مریض اور مسافر بعد میں پورا کرے، البتہ مسلسل رکھ کر جلدی پورا کرے تو بہتر ہے تا کہ واجب جلدی سے ساقط ہو جائے ۔اور اگر موخر کرتے کرتے دوسرا رمضان آ گیا تو دوسرے رمضان کا روزہ پہلے رکھے تا کہ یہ بھی قضا نہ ہو جائے ، اور پہلے رمضان کے روزے کو اس کے بعد رکھے، اور موخر کرنے کی وجہ سے کوئی فدیہ وغیرہ نہیں ہے، البتہ غیر معمولی تاخیر کرنے پر استغفار کرے۔
وجه: ( ا ) عن ابن عمر ان النبي ال الله قال في قضاء رمضان ان شاء فرق وان شاء تابع (دار قطنی ، باب القبلة للصائم ، ج ثانی ، ص ۷۴ انمبر ۲۳۰۸/۲۳۰۵) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ تفریق کر کے اور مسلسل رکھ کر قضا کرنا دونوں کی گنجائش ہے۔ (۲) تاخیر کر سکتا ہے اس کے لئے حدیث یہ ہے . سمعت عائشة تقول كان يكون على الصوم من رمضان فما استطيع أن اقضيه الا فى شعبان ۔ ( بخاری شریف، باب متنی یقضی قضاء رمضان ؟ ص ۳۱۳ نمبر ۱۹۵۰ / ابوداؤد شریف ، باب تاخیر قضاء رمضان ص ۳۴۸ نمبر ۲۳۹۹) (۳) اور تاخیر کرنے پر فدیہ لازم نہیں ہوگا اس کے لئے یہ اثر ہے۔ و قال ابراهيم : اذا
And continuity is not a condition in the qada of Ramadan. So when another Ramadan enters, its fasts are given precedence over qada, and no fidyah is due for delay. Translation: (116) And in qada, continuity is not a condition. So if another Ramadan arrives, its fasts should be given priority over qada, and there is no fidyah for delaying until the next Ramadan. Explanation: For example, ten days of Ramadan were missed. It is permissible to make them up consecutively on ten days, or it is permissible to fast two days, then after some days fast four, and complete the ten with intervals. Because the verse is absolute: the ill and traveler should complete later. However, it is better to make them up quickly so that the obligation is fulfilled soon. And if delaying leads to the next Ramadan, then the fasts of the new Ramadan should be done first so that they are not also missed, and then the qada of the previous Ramadan should be done. No fidyah is due for simply delaying, but for excessive delay, one should seek forgiveness. Evidence: (1) From Ibn Umar that the Prophet (ﷺ) said regarding qada of Ramadan: 'If he wishes, he may separate them, and if he wishes, he may make them consecutive.' (Dar Qutni). (2) Delay is permissible as per the hadith: I heard Aisha say: 'I used to have fasts to make up from Ramadan and could not do so until Sha'ban.' (Bukhari, Abu Dawud). (3) No fidyah for delay: The effect of Ibrahim: 'When...' [incomplete].
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with evidence regarding the permissibility of non-consecutive qada and the priority of the current Ramadan over previous qada. The matn states that continuity (tawali) is not a condition in qada of Ramadan, and when another Ramadan comes, its fasts take precedence. No fidyah is required for simply delaying qada until after the next Ramadan. The commentary explains that one may make up missed fasts consecutively or with intervals, as the Quranic wording is absolute. It recommends making them up quickly to fulfill the obligation, but if one procrastinates until the next Ramadan, the current Ramadan must be fasted first to avoid missing it as well. Delaying without excuse is makruh, and one should seek forgiveness (istighfar), but no fidyah is due. The proofs are the hadith of Ibn Umar allowing either method, the practice of Aisha who delayed qada until Sha'ban, and the athar of Ibrahim indicating no fidyah. This reflects the Hanafi view that qada is valid even with interruption, and that the obligation of the current Ramadan is more urgent.

Key Terms
تتابع (tawali): Consecutive sequence of fasting days without interruption.
تفريق (tafriq): Separating the qada fasts with intervals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a person misses 10 fasts of Ramadan and delays making them up until the next Ramadan arrives, what is the ruling?
A) He must pay fidyah for each day delayed.
B) He must fast the current Ramadan first and then make up the previous ones, with no fidyah for the delay.
C) He can only make up the missed fasts after the current Ramadan.
D) He has sinned and must fast two months.

Key Takeaway
Continuity is not required for qada of Ramadan; the current Ramadan takes priority, and no fidyah is due for delay.

Making Up Missed Fasts When Able: Obligation and Fidya
Ruling on obligatory qada and fidya upon death after ability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on qada of missed fasts when able and determine when fidya becomes wajib upon the heirs.

Core Idea
If you recover and can fast but don't, then die — your heirs must feed the poor for each missed day.

The obligation to make up missed Ramadan fasts does not end with death if the person had the ability to fast after recovery or residency. In this lesson, we will examine the Hanafi ruling that qada is wajib when possible, and if death intervenes, fidya compensates. The evidence from Quran and hadith establishes this as a binding duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is making up missed Ramadan fasts (qada). The ruling starts with the matn: 'make up what they are able to make up, to the extent of residence and health.'
The ruling applies only to days the person had ability to fast after the excuse ends.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites Quran 2:185: 'And whoever is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days.' This establishes the obligation of qada when one becomes able.
The imperative fíddatu indicates wujub in Hanafi usul.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith from Ubadah ibn Nusi states: if one recovers and does not make up qada until death, food (fidya) is given on his behalf. Distinguish this from continuous illness where no fidya is required.
The hadith is the basis for fidya becoming wajib on heirs.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete example
The author gives an example: a person misses 10 fasts due to illness, then recovers for 5 days but does not fast, then dies. Qada is wajib for 5 days; after death, heirs must pay fidya for those 5 days.
The same rule applies to a traveler who becomes resident.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and tarjih
Some other schools (like Shafi'i) may not require fidya. The Hanafi school relies on the explicit hadith and analogy to other debts.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position: fidya wajib for days the deceased had ability to make up.

Source Text

It has preceded (1115): 'And they make up what they are able to make up, to the extent of residence and health.' Translation: (115) And to the extent that one has the ability to make up fasts during residence and health, they should make up (the missed fasts of illness/travel). Explanation: For example, ten Ramadan fasts were missed due to illness. Then he recovered for five days and could make up fasts but did not, then died. So making up five fasts is necessary, because he had that much time. But after death he cannot make up, so it becomes necessary for the heirs to give fidya for those five fasts. The same applies to a traveler. Evidence (1): The verse says: 'And whoever is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:185). From this verse it is understood that if one cannot fast in Ramadan, then when able, he should fast. (2) The hadith: 'From Ubadah ibn Nusi? that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: Whoever is ill in Ramadan and remains ill until death, no food is given on his behalf. But if he recovers and does not make up (the fasts) until death, then food is given on his behalf.' (Musannaf Abdur Razzaq, chapter on the sick person in Ramadan and its qada, vol. 4, p. 182, no. 7665). From this hadith it is understood that one should make up the previous Ramadan's fasts, and if he could not, then the heirs will give fidya on his behalf, because he had the time for qada.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the obligation of making up missed Ramadan fasts when one becomes capable, and the consequence of death before fulfilling that obligation. The author begins by stating the ruling from the matn: one must make up as many fasts as they had the ability to make up, corresponding to the period of health or residence they enjoyed. The commentary then illustrates with an example: a person misses ten fasts due to illness, then recovers and has five days of health during which he could have fasted but did not, then dies. He is obligated to make up those five fasts; since he cannot after death, his heirs must give fidya (food charity) for each missed day. The same rule applies to a traveler who becomes a resident. The evidence first cites Quran 2:185, which implies that the missed fasts should be completed when able. Second, a hadith from Ubadah ibn Nusi? (likely Ubadah ibn al-Samit) distinguishes between continuous illness (no fidya) and recovery with ability to make up followed by death (fidya required). This establishes that the duty of qada is immediate upon ability, and if death intervenes, fidya compensates. The Hanafi position is clear: qada is wajib when possible, and fidya becomes wajib on the estate for days the deceased could have made up but did not.

Key Terms
قضا (qada): Making up a missed obligatory fast after its time has passed.
فدیہ (fidya): A compensatory payment of food (or cash equivalent) given to the poor for each missed obligatory fast that cannot be made up due to death or permanent incapacity.
مسافر (musafir): A traveler who is allowed to break the fast in Ramadan; here, the same qada rules apply upon becoming resident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Nothing, because the missed fasts are waived upon death.
B) They must make up all 5 fasts on his behalf.
C) They must give fidya (food) for 5 days.
D) They must give fidya only if he was wealthy.

Key Takeaway
Qada of missed fasts is wajib when able; if death occurs after ability, fidya compensates for the missed days.

Consecutive Fasting Not Required for Qada of Ramadan
Ruling that tawali is not a condition for qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consecutive fasting (tawali) in qada of Ramadan, cite the relevant proofs, and explain why no fidya is due for delay until the next Ramadan.

Core Idea
Consecutive fasting is meritorious but not required; you may make up missed fasts on separate days without penalty.

Many assume that missed Ramadan fasts must be made up consecutively or that delaying beyond a year incurs a penalty. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position using clear hadith evidence and the absolute nature of the Quranic command. You will see how flexibility is embedded in the texts and why the school prioritizes completing the current Ramadan over past qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening sentence: 'اور قضا میں لگا تار روزہ رکھنے کی شرط نہیں ہے' — consecutive fasting is not a condition. Note that the author immediately gives two permissible methods: consecutive or separated.
This is the main legal claim being proven.
2
Examine the implication for timing
Consider what happens if the next Ramadan arrives before qada is complete. The passage says to fast the current Ramadan first, then continue qada after. This shows that the obligation of the present takes precedence over the past.
This also addresses the question of fidya for delay.
3
Locate the first proof (permissibility)
The hadith from Ibn Umar (Dar Qutni) explicitly says 'if he wishes, he may separate; if he wishes, he may follow consecutively.' This directly supports the ruling that tawali is not wajib.
Note the wording: 'ان شاء فرق وان شاء تابع' — both options are allowed.
4
Locate the proof for delay without fidya
The hadith from Aisha (Bukhari) shows that she sometimes delayed qada until Sha‘ban, well after the next Ramadan had passed. The athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha‘i explicitly says there is no fidya for delay until the next Ramadan comes.
This refutes the idea that fidya is incurred automatically for delay.
5
Contrast with the recommendation for consecutiveness
Finally, the hadith from Abu Huraira recommends fasting consecutively: 'فليسرده ولا يقطعه' — let him fast them consecutively and not interrupt. This shows that tawali is mustahabb (better) but not obligatory, as the first hadith already allows separation.
The author thus reconciles the texts: tawali is superior but not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( دارقطنی ، باب القبلة للصائم ج ثانی ص اے انمبر ۲۲۸۹) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ منقطع نہ کرے بلکہ مسلسل روزہ رکھ کر جلدی اس کو پورا کرے۔
Translation: (116) And consecutive fasting is not required in qada. So if another Ramadan comes, he should prioritize its fast over qada, and there is no fidya for delaying (until the next Ramadan). Explanation: For example, ten Ramadan fasts were qada. It is permissible to fast ten consecutive days to complete them, and it is also permissible to fast two days, then some days later four days, and then the remaining days with intervals, completing ten days. Because the verse is absolute: the ill and traveler should complete later. However, fasting consecutively to complete quickly is better so that the obligation is discharged quickly. And if delay occurs until the next Ramadan, then he should fast the current Ramadan first so that it also does not become qada, and then fast the previous Ramadan's qada after that. No fidya is due for the delay, but for unusual delay he should seek forgiveness. Evidence: (1) From Ibn Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said regarding qada of Ramadan: 'If he wishes, he may separate, and if he wishes, he may follow consecutively.' (Dar Qutni) From this hadith it is understood that both consecutive and non-consecutive qada are permissible. (2) He may delay. The hadith for this: Aisha said: 'I would have fasts to make up from Ramadan and could not make them up except in Shaban.' (Bukhari, Abu Dawud) (3) And that fidya is not necessary for delay. The athar: Ibrahim said: 'When he makes up, if another Ramadan comes, he should prioritize the (current) Ramadan over qada, and there is no fidya for delay.' (It continues: 'And it is permissible for an old man and an old woman to break their fast, and fidya is obligatory on them: for each day half a sa' of wheat...' This part seems to be a textual overlap with the next ruling.) (4) However, fasting consecutively and quickly to be free of the obligation is better. Hadith from Abu Huraira: 'Whoever has fasts to make up from Ramadan, let him fast them consecutively and not break them.' (Dar Qutni) This hadith indicates not to interrupt but to fast consecutively and complete quickly.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that consecutive fasting (tawali) is not a condition for making up missed Ramadan fasts. The author explains that qada can be done in separate batches; the verse is general, so any number of days counted as qada is fine. However, it is better to fast consecutively to discharge the obligation sooner. If the next Ramadan arrives before completing qada, one must fast the current Ramadan first to avoid missing it, then continue qada. No fidya is due for delaying qada beyond one Ramadan, though a person should repent if the delay is excessive. The evidence includes a hadith from Ibn Umar allowing either consecutive or separate fasting, a hadith from Aisha showing that she sometimes delayed qada until Shaban, and an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i stating no fidya for delay. Another hadith from Abu Huraira recommends consecutive fasting as superior. The Hanafi position is clear: tawali is mustahabb, not wajib. The ruling that fidya is not due for delay aligns with the majority view. The passage also includes a brief mention of the elderly's exemption (which is treated in the next lesson), but the focus here is on the permissibility of separating qada and the lack of fidya for delay.

Key Terms
تتابع (tawali): Fasting consecutive days without interruption in between.
فدیہ (fidya): Compensation for missed fasts; here, not applicable for delaying qada within a year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on making up missed Ramadan fasts on separate days?
A) It is invalid; they must be consecutive.
B) It is permissible but must be completed before the next Ramadan.
C) It is permissible; the next Ramadan can be delayed but fidya is due afterwards.
D) It is permissible even if the next Ramadan comes before completion; no fidya is due, and the current Ramadan takes precedence.

Key Takeaway
Consecutive fasting for qada of Ramadan is recommended but not required; you may separate the days, and no fidya is owed for delay until the next Ramadan.

Elderly Person's Exemption: Paying Fidya
Ruling with proof for elderly person's exemption and fidya obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an elderly person who permanently cannot fast, explain the proof from Quran and the saying of Ibn Abbas, and apply the fidya obligation (half a sa' of wheat per day).

Core Idea
An elderly person who permanently cannot fast must pay fidya—half a sa' of wheat per missed day—and is not required to make up the fast.

Old age can bring physical weakness that makes fasting impossible. The Hanafi school addresses this with a clear ruling: the permanently incapacitated elderly are excused from fasting but must compensate by feeding a poor person for each missed day. This lesson unpacks the evidence from the Quran and the interpretation of Ibn Abbas that establishes this obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is a very old person (shaykh fani) who cannot fast and has no hope of recovery. The ruling: fasting is not required; instead, fidya is wajib for each missed day.
Fidya here is a financial compensation, not a makeup fast.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites the Quranic verse (2:184): 'And upon those who can bear it, a fidya: feeding a poor person.' The word 'yutequnahu' is interpreted as 'those who can barely bear it' i.e., the elderly.
The verse appears to address a general case; the interpretation specifies the elderly.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the verse prove the ruling? The phrase 'alladhina yutequnahu' is taken by Ibn Abbas to mean the elderly who cannot fast. The imperative to give fidya is understood as an obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul.
The Hanafi school holds that the verse is not abrogated, contrary to some other schools.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g. Shafi'i) consider the verse abrogated and hold that the elderly are simply excused without fidya. The Hanafi school maintains its applicability based on the saying of Ibn Abbas and other evidence.
This ikhtilaf has practical consequences: Shafi'is do not require fidya from the elderly; Hanafis do.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi reliance on the verse as non-abrogated and the explicit words of Ibn Abbas (recorded in authentic hadith collections) forms the basis for the mufta bihi position: fidya of half a sa' of wheat per day, no qada.
The amount (half a sa') is specified in the narration of Ibn Abbas.
