(ابوداؤد شریف، المعتلف یعود المریض ص ۳۴۲ نمبر ۲۴۷۳ / دار قطنی ، باب الاعتکاف ج ثانی ص ۱۸۱ نمبر ۲۳۳۹) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ ضرورت کے علاوہ کے لئے نہ نکلے، اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بغیر ضرورت کے نکلے گا تو اعتکاف فاسد ہو جائے گا۔ (۴) أخبرني على بن الحسين أن صفية زوج النبي الا الله أخبرته أنها جائت الى رسول الله مال الله تزوره في اعتکافه في المسجد في العشر الاواخر من رمضان فتحدثت عنده ساعة ثم قامت تنقلب فقام النبي عل الله معها يقلبها حتى اذا بلغت باب المسجد عند باب ام سلمة. الخ . ( بخاری شریف، باب هل يخرج المعتلف لحوائجه الی باب المسجد ؟ ، ص ۱۳۲۶، نمبر ۲۰۳۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حضور مسجد کے دروازے تک ہی گئے جس کا مطلب یہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ بغیر ضرورت کے اس سے باہر جانے سے اعتکاف فاسد ہو سکتا تھا۔ (۵) اس اثر میں ہے. قالت كانت عائشة في اعتكافها اذا خرجت الى بيتها لحاجتها ، تـمـر بـالـمـريـض فتسأل عنه و هى مجتازة لا تقف - (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب سنة الاعتکاف، ج رابع ، ص ۲۷۴،
Chapter of I'tikaf: For a religious need like Friday prayer or a natural need like urination (1136) or an emergency like the mosque collapsing or an oppressor forcibly expelling, and the dispersal of its people, and fear for oneself or one's property from the arrogant ones, then he enters another mosque immediately (1137). If he exits for an hour without an excuse, the obligatory (i'tikaf) becomes invalid, and other than it ends. (Hadith references). Translation: (1136) Or for an emergency necessity, like the mosque collapses or an oppressor forcibly expels him, or the residents of that mosque disperse so that congregational prayer is not held, or there is danger to life or property from oppressors, then he immediately enters another mosque. Explanation: Here it gives five examples of emergency necessities. When compulsion arises, it is permissible for the mu'takif to leave the mosque. [1] For example, if the mosque collapses, it is permissible to leave, but he must immediately proceed to another mosque; if he engages in another activity, the i'tikaf becomes invalid. [2] Or an oppressor forcibly expels the mu'takif. [3] Or the residents nearby have scattered such that congregational prayer is not held. [4] Or there is danger to life from oppressors, or fear of theft of property; in such compulsion, leaving is permissible, but he must go to another mosque immediately. Evidence: This hadith states that due to compulsion, the Prophet left the mosque. (Hadith from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). Vocabulary: Emergency necessity: a need that arises due to compulsion. Collapse: falling down. Dispersal: derived from 'furq' (separation), scattering. Arrogant: derived from 'kibr' (pride), oppressive people. Translation: (1137) If he exits even for one hour without any excuse, then obligatory i'tikaf becomes invalid, and other than it ends. Explanation: Without human necessity or compulsion, if he exits even for a moment, the i'tikaf ends. However, the opinion of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that if exits without necessity for half a day, then i'tikaf becomes invalid (Hidayah, Bab al-I'tikaf, issue 999). Vocabulary: 'Fasad al-wajib': For example, he vowed to perform i'tikaf for one month and exits on the fifth day, then the entire i'tikaf becomes invalid; the vow is not fulfilled, so he must start over for one month. 'Intaha bihi ghayruhu': Means it was not obligatory; for example, it was the Sunnah i'tikaf of the last ten days of Ramadan, and after six days he exits the mosque, then the six days of sunnah i'tikaf were performed, and the remaining i'tikaf ends. If he performs the remaining four days, they become separate, not joined to the first six, and the sunnah mu'akkadah of the last ten days is not fulfilled. Or, for example, he vowed to perform i'tikaf for the entire month of Rajab, and after twenty days he exits, causing i'tikaf to become invalid; then he performs the remaining ten days, so the i'tikaf of Rajab is fulfilled and the vow is completed, but due to invalidity the continuity is lost. Evidence: (1) The meaning of i'tikaf is staying in the mosque; and if he leaves without need, it contradicts i'tikaf, so analogy requires that i'tikaf becomes invalid. (2) Hadith from Aisha that the Prophet would pass by a sick person while in i'tikaf, walking as he was and not stopping to ask about him. This shows that stopping for long is not correct. Imam Abu Hanifa deduced from this that staying long without need invalidates i'tikaf. (3) This hadith indicates similarly: Aisha said that the sunnah for the mu'takif is not to visit the sick, nor attend funerals, nor touch a woman, nor have physical contact, nor go out for any need except what is unavoidable. This means that if he exits without need, i'tikaf becomes invalid. (4) Hadith from Bukhari that the Prophet accompanied Safiyyah to the door of the mosque, indicating that going beyond without need could invalidate i'tikaf. (5) Athar that Aisha, when in i'tikaf, would pass by a sick person while walking but would not stop.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs on the permissibility and invalidity of leaving the mosque during i'tikaf. The author begins by dividing permissible exits into three categories: religious needs (e.g., Friday prayer), natural needs (e.g., urination), and emergency necessities (e.g., mosque collapse, coercive expulsion, danger to life or property). For emergencies, the mu'takif must immediately proceed to another mosque; engaging in any other activity invalidates the i'tikaf. Five examples of emergency are given: mosque collapse, expulsion by oppressor, dispersal of congregation, fear for life, and fear for property. The evidence includes a hadith where the Prophet left the mosque due to compulsion.

Then the passage states the general rule: exiting without any excuse, even for an hour, invalidates wajib (vowed) i'tikaf, and terminates non-wajib (e.g., sunnah) i'tikaf. The Sahibayn differ, holding that only half a day of needless exit invalidates. This intra-Hanafi dispute is noted from al-Hidayah. The passage further explains the practical difference between wajib and non-wajib i'tikaf: for wajib (vowed), if one exits without excuse, the entire i'tikaf is invalidated and must be repeated from the beginning. For non-wajib (like sunnah of last ten days), exiting breaks only the remaining portion, and the past days count as performed; if one resumes, it is a new i'tikaf, not a continuation.

Finally, the author provides six proofs (wujuh) for Imam Abu Hanifa's position that any needless exit invalidates i'tikaf: (1) qiyas (i'tikaf means staying in mosque; leaving contradicts it), (2) hadith of Prophet not stopping to visit sick, (3) hadith that sunnah forbids visiting sick etc., (4) hadith of Prophet going only to mosque door, (5) athar of Aisha not stopping, and (6) the general principle that i'tikaf requires constant presence. All proofs emphasize that unnecessary exits break the i'tikaf.

Key Terms
اعتکاف (i'tikaf): Seclusion in the mosque with the intention of worship, staying in the mosque continuously.
مکابر (mukabir): Derived from kibr (pride); refers to an arrogant, oppressive person who uses force.
اضطراری (idtirari): An emergency necessity that leaves no choice, such as mosque collapse or threat to life.
صاحبين (sahibayn): The two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the main students of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), how long can a mu'takif exit the mosque without an excuse before the i'tikaf becomes invalid?
A) Any exit, even for an hour, invalidates i'tikaf.
B) Half a day of needless exit invalidates i'tikaf.
C) A full day of needless exit invalidates i'tikaf.
D) Exit without excuse never invalidates i'tikaf.

Key Takeaway
Exiting i'tikaf without a valid need invalidates the i'tikaf; emergency exits require immediate transfer to another mosque.

Ruling on Eating, Drinking, Sleeping, and Necessary Transactions in the Mosque for the Mu'takif
States the permissibility of basic needs for the mu'takif in the mosque with supporting hadiths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the Hanafi school permits a mu'takif to eat, drink, sleep, and conduct necessary transactions in the mosque, and identify the hadith proofs that support this ruling.

Core Idea
A mu'takif may eat, drink, sleep, and do necessary transactions in the mosque, proven by Sunnah.

I'tikaf involves staying in the mosque for devotion, but daily needs must be met. This lesson clarifies that basic activities like eating, drinking, sleeping, and even essential business transactions are permissible within the mosque for the mu'takif. The Hanafi school supports this with multiple hadiths from the Prophet's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on eating, drinking, sleeping, and necessary transactions for the mu'takif. The hukm level is mubah (permissible).
Necessary transactions means essential purchases for self or family, not idle trade.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites five proofs: hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar sleeping in mosque, Aisha washing Prophet's hair, Barira sale incident, Ka'b demanding debt, and Athar of Ata.
All are from Bukhari and Musannaf Abdurrazzaq.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For sleeping: practice of a companion without i'tikaf, so a fortiori for mu'takif. For eating/drinking: inferred from physical care in mosque. For transactions: explicit mention of sale and debt in mosque.
The 'illa is necessity and the nature of i'tikaf requiring presence in mosque.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage mentions no direct dispute but hints that non-mu'takifs should avoid such activities, and other schools may restrict transactions more strictly.
The mufta bihi is permissibility for mu'takif.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) as per later texts like Thamaratun Najah. The evidential weight from multiple hadiths supports permissibility.
The ruling is from fatawa mutakhkhirin, consistent with earlier Hanafi works.

Chapter of I'tikaf. (1138) The mu'takif's eating, drinking, sleeping, and concluding a sale for what he needs for himself or his family are in the mosque. (Number 8085) There is this effect that Aisha did not stay with a sick person, meaning that this could invalidate i'tikaf. Translation: (1138) And the mu'takif's eating, drinking, sleeping, and the transaction of sale which is necessary for himself or his family will be in the mosque. Explanation: Since the mu'takif will stay day and night in the mosque, his eating, drinking, sleeping, and any trade that is very necessary for his family will all be performed in the mosque. Proofs: (1) He can sleep in the mosque; regarding this there is a hadith: 'Abdullah ibn Umar informed me that he used to sleep in the mosque of the Prophet (peace be upon him) when he was a young unmarried man. (Bukhari, Chapter on Men Sleeping in the Mosque, p.76 no.440) This hadith states that Abdullah ibn Umar slept in the mosque without i'tikaf, so with i'tikaf it is more appropriate. (2) There is also in this hadith from Aisha: she said, 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) used not to lower his head while he was in seclusion in the mosque, so I would comb his hair while I was menstruating.' (Bukhari, Chapter on the Menstruating Woman Combing the Head of the Mu'takif, p.1325 no.2028) This shows that the mu'takif can eat and drink in the mosque. (3) For necessary trade work in the mosque, there is a hadith: Aisha said that Barira came to her asking her to help her with her contract of emancipation. So the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Buy her and free her, for wala is for the one who frees.' Then he stood on the pulpit and said, 'What is the matter with people who stipulate conditions that are not in the Book of Allah?' (Bukhari, Chapter on Mentioning Sale and Purchase on the Pulpit in the Mosque, p.79 no.456) This shows that the mu'takif can discuss buying and selling. (4) From Ka'b: that he demanded from Ibn Abi Hadrad a debt that was due to him in the mosque, and their voices became raised until the Prophet (peace be upon him) heard them while he was in his house. (Bukhari, Chapter on Demanding Debt and Held in the Mosque, p.79 no.457) This shows that one can discuss demanding debt in the mosque, so if needed, the mu'takif can do such things, but making it a habit is not good. (5) There is also this effect: I said to Ata... 'Can a mu'takif buy and sell in the mosque?' He said, 'There is no problem with that.' (Musannaf Abdurrazzaq, Chapter on the Mu'takif Buying and Seeking Worldly Things, vol.4 p.278 no.8108) This shows that buying and selling is permissible for the mu'takif. However, for the non-mu'takif, this is not good; its proof will come later.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the permissibility of a mu'takif (one in i'tikaf) engaging in basic life activities within the mosque. The ruling explicitly states that eating, drinking, sleeping, and necessary business transactions (such as buying essentials for himself or his family) are to be done in the mosque. The author provides five proofs from the Sunnah. First, from the practice of Abdullah ibn Umar, who slept in the mosque without being in i'tikaf, so a mu'takif has even more reason to do so. Second, from Aisha's act of washing the Prophet's hair in the mosque during his i'tikaf, indicating that eating and drinking are also permissible. Third, from the incident of Barira where the Prophet spoke about a sale on the pulpit, showing that discussing trade in the mosque is allowed. Fourth, from the story of Ka'b demanding a debt in the mosque, showing that financial transactions can be mentioned. Fifth, from the effect of Ata, who explicitly permitted a mu'takif to buy and sell in the mosque. The author notes that while these activities are permissible for the mu'takif, making them a habit is discouraged, and for non-mu'takifs, they are not recommended. This ruling is based on the principle that i'tikaf requires staying in the mosque, and these activities are necessary for daily life, so they are allowed within the confines of the mosque.

Key Terms
معتكف (mu'takif): A person who is performing i'tikaf, a devotional seclusion in the mosque.
اعتكاف (i'tikaf): Staying in the mosque with the intention of devotion, away from worldly distractions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Conducting a major business deal for profit.
B) Eating, drinking, and sleeping.
C) Engaging in loud arguments.
D) Staying silent as a form of worship.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a mu'takif to fulfill basic needs and necessary transactions within the mosque, based on prophetic practice, ensuring i'tikaf does not cause undue hardship.

Ruling on Bringing Trade Goods and Conducting Business in the Mosque
Ruling on commercial activities in the mosque from Thamaratun Najah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bringing trade goods and conducting business transactions in the mosque, identify the proof from hadith, and apply the distinction between necessary and unnecessary transactions.

Core Idea
Bringing trade goods into a mosque is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrīmi) unless necessary.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling regarding commercial activities in the mosque. While the mosque is a place of worship, scholars have addressed the permissibility of trade within its walls. This passage, from Thamaratun Najah, declares that bringing goods for sale and conducting business transactions without necessity is makruh tahrīmi. We will see the textual evidence and understand the nuance that necessity changes the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what actions are being ruled upon: bringing trade goods into the mosque and conducting business transactions. Note the ruling: makruh (disliked) when done without necessity.
The text specifically addresses the mu'takif (one in i'tikaf), but the ruling applies generally.
2
Examine the proof
Locate the two hadiths cited. Identify the key phrases: 'Keep your mosques free from your buying and selling' and 'He forbade buying and selling in the mosque.' Consider the chain of transmission and the strength of the evidence.
The hadith are ahad (single narration) but are considered sufficient for rulings of prohibition.
3
Understand the classification
Why is this makruh tahrīmi (prohibitively disliked) rather than haram? The Hanafi school interprets the command as a strong discouragement but not absolute prohibition, because there is an exception for necessity. Also, the previous lesson allowed necessary transactions, confirming that the default is dislike, not haram.
Karāha tahrīmiyya is close to haram but permits concession in cases of need.
4
Note the exception for necessity
The text says 'without necessity' (bighayr al-darura). Necessity permits transactions; the dislike is lifted when there is a genuine need. This is a key nuance – the ruling is not absolute.
Necessity is assessed on a case-by-case basis; a mu'takif's genuine need for a transaction is allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۱۳۹) بیچ کی چیز کا مسجد میں لانا مکروہ ہے۔ اور بغیر ضرورت کے تجارت کی چیزوں کا معاملہ کرنا مکروہ ہے۔ (۱۱۳۹) وكره احضار المبيع فيه وكره عقد ما كان للتجارة تشریح : مختلف کے لئے مسجد میں بیج کے سامان کالا نا مکروہ ہے، کیونکہ اس سے اعتکاف کے بجائے تجارت کا ماحول بن جائے گا۔ اور ضرورت نہ ہوتو خرید وفروخت کی بات کرنا بھی مکروہ ہے۔ وجه : (۱) حدیث میں ہے ۔ عن واثلة بن الأسقع أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : جنبوا مساجدكم صبيانكم، و مجانينكم ، و شراركم ، و بيعكم ، و خصوماتكم ، و رفع اصواتكم و اقامة حدود كم، و سل سيوفكم ، و اتخذوا على ابوابها المطاهر، و جمروها في الجمع - (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب ما يكره في المساجد ، ص ۱۰۷ نمبر ۷۵۰) (۲) دوسری حدیث میں ہے . عن عمرو ابن شعيب عن ابيه عن جده أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن الشراء و البيع في المسجد ۔(ابوداود شریف، باب التخلق يوم الجمعة قبل الصلوۃ ص ۱۶۳ نمبر ۰۷۹ ارترندی شریف ، باب ما جاء في كراهية البيع والشراء وانشاد الضالة والشعر في المسجد ، ص ۸۸، نمبر ۳۲)
Translation: (1139) Bringing the commodity being sold into the mosque is makruh, and without necessity, conducting trade transactions is makruh. (1139) And it is makruh to bring the sold item into it, and makruh to conclude a transaction that is for trade. Explanation: It is makruh for the mu'takif to bring trade goods into the mosque, because this would create an atmosphere of trade instead of i'tikaf. And if there is no need, speaking about buying and selling is also makruh. Proofs: (1) In a hadith: From Wathila ibn al-Asqa' that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Keep your mosques free from your children, your insane, your wicked, your buying and selling, your disputes, raising your voices, implementing legal punishments, and drawing your swords. And place vessels for purification at their doors, and perfume them on Fridays.' (Ibn Majah, Chapter on What is Disliked in Mosques, p.107 no.750) (2) Another hadith: From Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade buying and selling in the mosque. (Abu Dawud, Chapter on Adornment on Friday before Prayer, p.163 no.079; Tirmidhi, Chapter on What is Disliked about Buying and Selling and Announcing Lost Items and Poetry in the Mosque, p.88 no.32)
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that it is makruh (disliked) for a mu'takif to bring trade goods into the mosque and to conduct business transactions without necessity. The author explains that the reason is that such activities change the atmosphere from one of devotion to one of commerce. Two hadiths are cited as proof: one listing prohibitions regarding mosques, including buying and selling, and another explicitly prohibiting buying and selling in the mosque. This ruling applies primarily to the mu'takif, but by implication also to others, though the text focuses on the mu'takif. It is important to note that in the previous lesson, necessary transactions were allowed; here, unnecessary trade is discouraged. Thus, the mu'takif should limit worldly activities to genuine needs.

Key Terms
كراهة تحريمية (karāha tahrīmiyya): Prohibitively disliked; a deed that is close to haram and should be avoided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is permissible (mubah).
B) It is mandatory (wajib).
C) It is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrīmi).
D) It is forbidden (haram).

Key Takeaway
Bringing trade goods or conducting business in the mosque without necessity is makruh tahrīmi, based on prophetic prohibitions, but necessity renders it permissible.

Ruling on Silence and Speaking in I'tikaf
Ruling with proof on silence and speech in i'tikaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on remaining silent during i'tikaf and derive the ruling from the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Silence as worship is makruh in i'tikaf; speaking good is encouraged.

A common misconception is that a mu'takif must remain silent. The Hanafi school clarifies that silence is not an act of worship. Instead, speaking good is encouraged. This lesson examines the ruling and its proof from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The text rules on two related acts: remaining silent with the intention of worship, and speaking except for good. Note the hukm level: makruh (disliked).
The ruling applies specifically within i'tikaf.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? A hadith from Bukhari and Abu Dawud: the Prophet (peace be upon him) conversed with Safiyya during his i'tikaf.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but its meaning is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove the ruling? The Prophet's action of talking indicates that speech is permissible. If silence were a required devotion, he would not have spoken. Therefore, considering silence as worship is contrary to the Sunnah.
The evidence is from the Prophet's practice (fi'l).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some earlier Muslims considered silence a form of devotion, but the Hanafi school rejects this based on the hadith.
The disagreement is not with another madhhab but with a practice of some ascetics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱۴۰) وكره الصمت ان اعتقده قربة والتكلم الا بخير تشریح مستقل چپ رہنا اسلام میں عبادت نہیں ہے اس لئے عبادت کے طور پر چپ رہنا مکروہ ہے۔ خیر کی بات کرنی چاہئے۔ وجه (۱) حدیث میں ہے ۔ أخبرني علی بن الحسين أن صفية زوج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أخبرته أنها جائت الى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم تزوره في اعتكافه في المسجد في العشر الاواخر من رمضان فتحدثت عنده ساعة ثم قامت تنقلب فقام النبى صلى الله عليه وسلم معها يقلبها حتى اذا بلغت باب المسجد عند باب ام سلمة. الخ. (بخارى شریف ، باب هل يخرج المعتكف لحوائجه الى باب المسجد ؟ ، ص ،۳۲۶، نمبر ۲۰۳۵ / ابو داؤد شریف ،المعتلك يدخل البيت لحاجته ص ۳۴۲٫۳۴۱ نمبر۲۴۷۰)
(1140) And it is makruh to remain silent if he considers it a devotional act, and to speak except for good. Explanation: Absolute silence is not worship in Islam, so remaining silent as a form of worship is makruh. One should speak good. Proof: (1) In a hadith: 'Ali ibn al-Husain informed me that Safiyya, the wife of the Prophet (peace be upon him), told him that she came to visit the Prophet (peace be upon him) during his i'tikaf in the mosque in the last ten days of Ramadan. She talked with him for a while, then stood up to leave. The Prophet (peace be upon him) stood up with her to accompany her until when she reached the door of the mosque at the door of Umm Salama.' (Bukhari, Chapter on Does the Mu'takif Go Out for His Needs to the Door of the Mosque?, p.326 no.2035; Abu Dawud, Chapter on the Mu'takif Entering the House for His Needs, p.341-342 no.2470)
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on silence during i'tikaf. The Hanafi school holds that remaining silent with the intention that it is an act of worship is makruh (disliked). This is because Islam encourages speaking good words and discourages silence as a means of devotion. The author explains that absolute silence is not recognized as worship. Instead, the mu'takif should occupy himself with good speech, such as remembrance of Allah, recitation of Quran, or beneficial talk. The proof comes from an incident where the Prophet (peace be upon him) accompanied Safiyya to the door of the mosque while in i'tikaf, demonstrating that he engaged in conversation (good speech) during his i'tikaf. This indicates that speaking good is permissible, while silence considered as worship is not.

Key Terms
صمت (samt): Silence; abstaining from speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a mu'takif remaining silent as an act of worship?
A) It is recommended (sunnah).
B) It is permissible (mubah).
C) It is disliked (makruh).
D) It is forbidden (haram).

Key Takeaway
Remaining silent as a devotional act during i'tikaf is makruh; instead, one should speak good words as demonstrated by the Prophet's practice.

The Prohibition of Sexual Intercourse and Its Preliminaries During I'tikaf
Declares sexual intercourse and its preliminaries haram during i'tikaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sexual intercourse and its preliminaries during i'tikaf and identify the Quranic evidence for this prohibition.

Core Idea
Both sexual intercourse and any intimate contact are haram during i'tikaf, as per the explicit Quranic prohibition.

This lesson addresses a clear ruling from the Hanafi school regarding actions that nullify the sanctity of i'tikaf. The passage cites the Quranic verse from Surah al-Baqarah to establish that not only intercourse but also its preliminaries are forbidden. Understanding this ruling helps the mu'takif maintain the validity of his retreat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules that sexual intercourse (wat') and its preliminaries (dawa'i) such as touching and kissing are haram during i'tikaf.
The prohibition is absolute, applying both day and night.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the Quranic verse (Surah al-Baqarah 2:187), which explicitly prohibits sexual contact.
The verse uses the phrase 'la tabashiruhunna'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The term 'mubashara' includes both intercourse and intimate touching, as it literally means 'to have contact'. The imperative prohibition indicates a definitive ruling of haram.
Hanafi usul: al-amr bi al-tahrim yufidu al-tahrim.
4
Note the scope of the ruling
The prohibition applies throughout the entire duration of i'tikaf, including night, because i'tikaf is continuous.
The text explicitly mentions that i'tikaf occurs at night as well.
5
Evaluate the consequence of violation
Committing any of these prohibited acts intentionally invalidates the i'tikaf.
This is the unanimous Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱۴۱) وحرم الوطئ و دواعيه تشریح : اعتکاف کی حالت میں وطی کرنا ، یا دوائی ولی یعنی عورت کو چھونا بوسہ لینا حرام ہے، اور اعتکاف رات میں بھی ہوتا ہے اس لئے رات میں بھی ایسا کرنا حرام ہے۔ وجه : (۱) آیت یہ ہے۔ ولا تباشروهن وانتم عاكفون في المساجد . ( آیت ۱۸۷ سورۃ البقرۃ ۲)
(1141) And sexual intercourse and its preliminaries are forbidden. Explanation: During the state of i'tikaf, intercourse, or its preliminaries such as touching a woman and kissing are haram. I'tikaf also occurs at night, so doing such things at night is also haram. Proof: (1) The verse is: 'And do not have relations with them (your wives) while you are in retreat in the mosques.' (Quran 2:187)
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of sexual activity during i'tikaf. The Hanafi school rules that sexual intercourse (wat') and its preliminaries (dawa'i) such as touching, kissing, and any intimate contact are absolutely haram (forbidden) during i'tikaf. This prohibition applies both day and night, as i'tikaf is continuous. The primary evidence is the Quranic verse (Surah al-Baqarah 2:187) which explicitly prohibits 'mubashara' (sexual contact) while in i'tikaf in the mosques. The term 'mubashara' in this verse is understood by Hanafi scholars to include both intercourse and any intimate touching that could lead to it. The ruling is clear and decisive, as the verse uses the imperative prohibition. The mu'takif must avoid all such actions to maintain the validity of his i'tikaf.

Key Terms
وطء (wat'): Sexual intercourse.
دواعي الوطء (dawa'i al-wat'): Actions that lead to or are preliminaries of intercourse, such as touching, kissing, and fondling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a mu'takif kissing his wife during i'tikaf?
A) It is permissible (mubah).
B) It is disliked (makruh) but does not invalidate i'tikaf.
C) It is forbidden (haram) and invalidates i'tikaf.
D) It is recommended (sunnah).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly prohibits all forms of intimate contact during i'tikaf, based on the Quranic verse, to preserve the sanctity of the retreat.

Vowing Days and Nights of I'tikaf: Inclusion and Exclusion
Rulings on vows of I'tikaf days and nights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the inclusion of days when vowing nights of I'tikaf, and identify when explicit exclusion overrides the default inclusion.

Core Idea
Vowing nights of I'tikaf automatically obligates the days consecutively, unless explicitly excluded at the time of the vow.

The Hanafi school holds that in common Arabic usage, 'day' includes the following night and 'night' includes the preceding day. This principle governs the interpretation of vows (nadhr) for I'tikaf. However, the person making the vow can override this default by explicitly excluding the complementary period at the time of vowing. The passage from Thamaratun Najah clarifies these rules with examples and an athar from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default inclusion rule
Recognize that in Hanafi fiqh, due to 'urf (common usage), vowing nights automatically obligates the associated days, and vowing days obligates the associated nights, consecutively, unless otherwise stated.
This is the principle from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Apply the rule to vowing nights
If a person vows five nights of I'tikaf, the five days between them become obligatory as well, and they must be performed consecutively.
The exception: if he explicitly rejects consecutiveness at the time of the vow, then it's not required.
3
Apply the rule to vowing days
Similarly, vowing two days obligates the two nights between them (the nights that follow each day).
This mirrors the first ruling.
4
Identify the effect of explicit exclusion
If the person explicitly excludes the night (e.g., says 'I will not do I'tikaf at night') when vowing days, then only the days are obligatory. The same applies to vowing a month: unless explicit exclusion, both day and night are included.
Explicit exclusion (istithna) overrides the default inclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولزمته الايام بنذر الليالي متتابعة وان يشترط التتابع فى ظاهر الرواية (۱۱۴۵) ولزمته ليلتان بنذر يومين (۱۱۴۶) وصح نية النهر خاصة دون الليالي (۱۱۴۷) وان نذر اعتکاف شهر و نوا النُّهُر بالليل قال ليس هذا باعتكاف (مصنف ابن ابی هیه ۸۷ ما قالوافی المعتلف یا تی اہلہ بالنهار، ج ثانی، ص ۳۳۶ نمبر ۹۶۴۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ دن کے ساتھ رات بھی شامل ہوگی۔ اور جب رات شامل ہوگی تو پے در پے ہو جائے گی ۔ ترجمه : (۱۱۴۴) اور راتوں کی منت سے دنوں کا لازم ہو جائے گا، لگا تارا گر چہ تابع کی شرط نہ لگائی ہو، ظاہری روایت میں۔ تشریح : کسی نے پانچ راتوں کے اعتکاف کی منت مانی تو انکے درمیان جو پانچ دن ہیں ان کا اعتکاف بھی لازم ہو جائے گا، اور ظاہری روایت میں پیدر پے کرنا ہوگا۔ لیکن یہ اس صورت میں ہے جبکہ باضابطہ پیدر پے کا انکار نہ کیا ہو۔ اس لئے اگر نذر مانتے وقت پیدرپے کا انکار کیا تو پھر پیدر پے لازم نہیں ہوگا۔

ترجمه : (۱۱۴۵) دو دن کی منت سے دورات کا (اعتکاف بھی لازم ہوگا۔ تشریح : دودنوں کے اعتکاف کی منت مانی تو اس کے ساتھ دورا تیں بھی لازم ہوں گیں، کیونکہ محاورے میں دن کے ساتھ رات بھی آتی ہے۔ ترجمه : (۱۱۴۶) اور خاص دن کی نیست رات کی نیت کے بغیر بھی صحیح ہے۔ تشریح : دن میں اعتکاف کی نذر مانتے وقت با ضابطہ رات کے اعتکاف کا انکار کیا تو یہ صحیح ہے ، اور اب صرف دن ہی کا اعتکاف لازم ہوگا۔ یوم کا معنی اگر چہ دن رات کے مجموعے کا ہے لیکن یہاں رات کو باضابطہ خارج کیا اس لئے اب رات کا اعتکاف لازم نہیں ہوگا۔۔: النصر : نہار کی جمع ہے، دن۔ : پر با کی نیت بینیت ترجمه : (۱۴۷) اگر ایک مہینہ کے اعتکاف کی منت مانی اور خاص طور پر دن کی یا مخصوص طور پر رات کی نیت کی تو یہ نیت مفید نہیں، مگر یہ کہ صراحۃ استثناء کر دے۔ تشریح : ایک مہینہ کے لفظ میں رات اور دن دونوں آتے ہیں اس لئے اس سے صرف دن مراد لی ، یا صرف رات مراد لی تو نہیں مانی جائے گی اور رات اور دن دونوں میں اعتکاف لازم ہوگا ۔ لیکن اگر یہاں بھی باضابطہ رات کا انکار کر دے کہ میں رات میں اعتکاف نہیں کروں گا تو رات میں اعتکاف لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ یا دن کا انکار کر دے کہ دن میں اعتکاف نہیں کروں گا تو صرف رات کا

اصول : مہینے کے لفظ میں رات اور دن دنوں شامل ہیں ، البتہ صراحت کے ساتھ انکار کرنے سے انکار ہو جائے گا۔
Chapter of I'tikaf. (1144) And the days become obligatory by vowing the nights, consecutively even if he does not stipulate consecutiveness in the apparent narration. (1145) And two nights become obligatory by vowing two days. (1146) And the intention of only the day without the nights is valid. (1147) And if he vows I'tikaf of a month and intends the day specifically or the night specifically, he said: this is not I'tikaf. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah 87: what they said about the mu'takif... this athar shows that the night is included with the day. And when the night is included, it becomes consecutive. Translation: (1144) And vowing nights makes days obligatory consecutively even if the condition of consecutiveness is not stipulated, in the apparent narration. Explanation: If someone vows five nights of I'tikaf, the five days between them also become obligatory, and in the apparent narration it must be consecutive. But this is when he did not explicitly reject consecutiveness. So if at the time of the vow he rejected consecutiveness, then consecutiveness is not obligatory. Reasoning: It passed above that in common usage the night is included with the day, so the day is also included with the night. Translation: (1145) Vowing two days obligates two nights' I'tikaf. Explanation: If he vows two days of I'tikaf, the two nights between them also become obligatory, because in common usage the night comes with the day. Translation: (1146) And intending only the day without the night is valid. Explanation: If at the time of vowing I'tikaf during the day he explicitly rejects I'tikaf at night, it is valid, and only the day's I'tikaf is obligatory. The meaning of 'yawm' is the combination of day and night, but here the night is explicitly excluded, so I'tikaf at night is not obligatory. 'Al-Nahar' is the plural of 'nahr', meaning day. (1147) If he vows I'tikaf of a month and specifically intends only the day or only the night, this intention is not effective unless he explicitly excludes. Explanation: The word 'month' includes both night and day, so if he means only the day or only the night, it is not accepted, and I'tikaf is obligatory for both nights and days. But if here too he explicitly rejects the night, saying 'I will not do I'tikaf at night', then I'tikaf at night is not obligatory. Or if he rejects the day, then only the night's I'tikaf is obligatory. Principle: The word 'month' includes both night and day; however, explicit exclusion takes effect.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on I'tikaf. It presents Hanafi rulings regarding vows (nadhr) for I'tikaf, specifically how vowing days or nights affects each other, and the role of intention and stipulation. The author bases these rulings on the principle that in common Arabic usage ('urf), 'day' (yawm) includes the night that follows, and 'night' includes the day that precedes it. Therefore, vowing nights automatically obligates the associated days, and vowing days obligates the associated nights. However, this inclusion can be negated by explicit exclusion (istithna) at the time of the vow. The text also discusses the effect of intending only part of a period. These rulings are from Zahir al-Riwayah, the most authoritative transmission within the Hanafi school. The passage includes an athar from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah as evidence for the inclusion of night with day. The student should note the distinction between the default ruling (luzum with ta'qib) and the effect of explicit rejection.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship; makes the act obligatory.
تتابع (tataabu'): Consecutiveness; performing I'tikaf without interruption.
اعتکاف (i'tikaf): Seclusion in the mosque for worship, especially during the last ten days of Ramadan.
استثناء (istithna'): Explicit exclusion of a part of the vowed time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only the two days
B) The two days and the two nights between them
C) The two days and one night
D) Nothing because the vow is incomplete

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh: Vowing I'tikaf for days or nights automatically includes the complementary periods consecutively, unless explicit exclusion is made at the time of the vow.

The Legal Basis and Virtues of I'tikaf
States the Islamic legitimacy and spiritual benefits of I'tikaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the proofs for I'tikaf from the Quran and Sunnah and explain its spiritual benefits.

Core Idea
I'tikaf is a sunnah mu'akkadah proven by Quran, Sunnah, and the practice of the Prophet.

This lesson explores the legislation of I'tikaf — a spiritual retreat in the mosque. You will learn that it is not merely a recommended act but one rooted in clear Quranic verses, prophetic practice, and the example of the righteous. Understanding its proofs and virtues will deepen your appreciation and help you observe it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text establishes I'tikaf as a legislated act (mashru') and states it is among the most noble deeds when done with sincerity. The ruling here is sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized sunnah).
It becomes wajib if vowed (nadhr).
2
Locate the primary dalil from the Quran
The author cites Surah al-Baqarah 2:187, which prohibits marital relations during I'tikaf, implying its existence as a known worship. Also 2:125 mentions 'al-akifin' (those staying for worship), showing its continuity.
Both verses are qat'i al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
From 2:187, the prohibition of normal marital activity while in I'tikaf indicates that I'tikaf is a recognized act of devotion with specific rules. This establishes its mashru'iyyah.
The verse does not command I'tikaf but regulates it, confirming its validity.
4
Note the Sunnah proof
The hadith from 'A'isha (r.a) shows that the Prophet consistently performed I'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan. Continuous practice (muwazabah) indicates sunnah mu'akkadah.
This hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim, agreed upon (muttafaq 'alayh).
5
Understand the spiritual benefits
The text describes I'tikaf as emptying the heart from worldly matters, submitting to Allah, and seeking forgiveness persistently, as illustrated by the example of a persistent supplicant.
Sincerity (ikhlas) is emphasized for full reward.

Translation (1148): The legislation of I'tikaf is established by the Book and Sunnah. It is among the most noble deeds when it is with sincerity. (1148) And I'tikaf is legislated by the Book and Sunnah, and it is among the most noble deeds when it is with sincerity. (1149) And among its virtues is that it empties the heart from worldly matters, submitting the self to the Lord, clinging to His worship in His house, and fortifying oneself in His fortress. 'Ata' said: The example of the mu'takif is like a man who repeatedly comes to the door of a great one for a need. The mu'takif says: I will not leave until He forgives me. And this is what is facilitated for the weak and lowly by the care of his Mighty and Powerful Lord. Praise be to Allah who guided us to this, and we would not have been guided if Allah had not guided us. And may Allah bless our Master Muhammad, the seal of the prophets, and his family, companions, and progeny. And we ask Allah, the Exalted, through means to make it purely for His noble face, and to benefit with it abundantly, and to give immense reward. Explanation: The meaning of I'tikaf is to sit at Allah's door saying 'I will not be driven away', and to free the heart from all worries and sit in Allah's house, so this deed is very virtuous. Reasoning: (1) I'tikaf is proven from this verse: 'And do not have relations with them while you are staying for worship in the mosques. These are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them.' (2:187) (2) 'And We made a covenant with Ibrahim and Isma'il to purify My House for those who circumambulate, those who stay for worship, and those who bow and prostrate.' (2:125) This verse is evidence for performing I'tikaf. (3) This hadith also is evidence for I'tikaf: From 'A'isha, wife of the Prophet, that the Prophet used to perform I'tikaf during the last ten days of Ramadan until Allah took his soul, then his wives performed I'tikaf after him. (Bukhari, chapter on I'tikaf in the last ten days, p. 271, no. 2026; Muslim, book of I'tikaf, p. 371, no. 2784/1172) This hadith shows that the Prophet always performed I'tikaf. Translation (1149): Among the virtues of I'tikaf is that it empties the heart, submits the self to the Lord, and commits to worship in His house, and one becomes safe in His fortress. 'Ata' said: The example of the mu'takif is like a man who has a need and goes to the door of a great person; the mu'takif says: I will not leave until my forgiveness is granted. Reasoning: This statement of a Tabi'i is evidence. From 'Uthman ibn 'Ata' from his father: He said: The example of the mu'takif is like a muhrim (one in ihram) who casts himself before the Merciful and says: By Allah, I will not leave until You have mercy on me. (Shu'ab al-Iman, chapter on I'tikaf, vol. 3, p. 426, no. 3970) This statement of a Tabi'i says: The mu'takif is like one who enters ihram and throws himself before the Merciful, saying: I will not go until You show mercy.

Commentary
This passage establishes the legitimacy and virtue of I'tikaf. The author cites Quranic verses (2:187 and 2:125) and a hadith from Bukhari and Muslim showing the Prophet's constant practice of I'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan. These proofs indicate that I'tikaf is a sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized sunnah) during the year, and especially meritorious in Ramadan. The passage also describes the spiritual benefits of I'tikaf: it empties the heart from worldly distractions, allows the servant to devote himself fully to Allah, and, as quoted from the Tabi'i 'Ata', the mu'takif is like a persistent supplicant at the door of a king, refusing to leave until forgiven. The author emphasizes that these benefits are achieved when I'tikaf is done with sincerity (ikhlas). This section is a commentary expanding on the matn that simply states the legislation and virtue.

Key Terms
مشروع (mashru'): Legislated; established by Islamic law.
اخلاص (ikhlas): Sincerity; doing the deed solely for Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a proof cited for the legislation of I'tikaf?
A) Surah al-Baqarah 2:187
B) Surah al-Baqarah 2:125
C) Hadith of the Prophet's I'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan
D) Ijma' (consensus) of the scholars

Key Takeaway
I'tikaf is a Sunnah mu'akkadah established by Quran, Sunnah, and prophetic practice, emphasizing spiritual devotion and sincerity.
